</div></td></tr><tr class="note"><td class="infobox-full-data" colspan="2"><div class="mw-collapsible mw-collapsed" style="text-align: center; font-size: 95%;">
<div style="line-height: 1.6em; font-weight: bold; font-size: 100%; text-align: left;"><div>Influenced</div></div>
<ul class="mw-collapsible-content" style="font-size: 105%; margin-top: 0; margin-bottom: 0; line-height: inherit; text-align: left; list-style: none; margin-left: 0;"><li style="line-height: inherit; margin: 0"><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">
<p>Virtually all of subsequent <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_philosophy" title="Middle Eastern philosophy">Middle Eastern</a> <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonism</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelianism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Academy" title="Old Academy">Old Academy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Academic_skepticism" title="Academic skepticism">Academic skepticism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Platonism" title="Middle Platonism">Middle Platonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism_and_Christianity" title="Neoplatonism and Christianity">Christian Platonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chaldean_Oracles" title="Chaldean Oracles">Chaldean Oracles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermeticism" title="Hermeticism">Hermeticism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonism_in_Islamic_Philosophy" title="Platonism in Islamic Philosophy">Islamic Platonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_philosophy" title="Byzantine philosophy">Byzantine philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy_(Florence)" title="Platonic Academy (Florence)">Florentine Academy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Platonism_in_the_Renaissance" title="Platonism in the Renaissance">Renaissance Platonism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Platonists" title="Cambridge Platonists">Cambridge Platonism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modern_Platonism" title="Modern Platonism">Modern Platonism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditionalist_School" title="Traditionalist School">Traditionalist School</a>
</p>
</div></div></li></ul>

<div class="CategoryTreeTag" data-ct-mode="20" data-ct-options='{"mode":20,"hideprefix":20,"showcount":false,"namespaces":false,"notranslations":false}'><div class="CategoryTreeSection"><div class="CategoryTreeItem"><span class="CategoryTreeBullet"><span class="CategoryTreeToggle" data-ct-state="collapsed" data-ct-title="Plato"></span> </span> <a href="/wiki/Category:Plato" title="Category:Plato">Plato</a></div><div class="CategoryTreeChildren" style="display:none"></div></div></div></td>

<ul><li><img alt="" class="noviewer" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="326" decoding="async" height="16" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/10px-Socrates.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/15px-Socrates.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cd/Socrates.png/21px-Socrates.png 2x" width="10"/> <a href="/wiki/Portal:Philosophy" title="Portal:Philosophy">Philosophy portal</a></li></ul></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Platonism" title="Template:Platonism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Platonism" title="Template talk:Platonism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Platonism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Plato</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'p' in 'pie'">p</span><span title="'l' in 'lie'">l</span><span title="/eɪ/: 'a' in 'face'">eɪ</span><span title="'t' in 'tie'">t</span><span title="/oʊ/: 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> <a href="/wiki/Help:Pronunciation_respelling_key" title="Help:Pronunciation respelling key"><i title="English pronunciation respelling"><span style="font-size:90%">PLAY</span>-toe</i></a>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJones2006_2-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJones2006-2">[2]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CE%A0%CE%BB%CE%AC%CF%84%CF%89%CE%BD" title="wikt:Πλάτων">Πλάτων</a></span></span> <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn">Plátōn</i></span>; 428/427 or 424/423 – 348/347 BC) was a <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Greek</a> <a href="/wiki/Philosopher" title="Philosopher">philosopher</a> born in <a href="/wiki/Athens" title="Athens">Athens</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Greece" title="Classical Greece">Classical period</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Ancient Greece</a>. He founded the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonist" title="Platonist">Platonist</a> school of thought and the <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Academy</a>, the first institution of higher learning on the European continent.
</p><p>Along with his teacher, <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>, and his student, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, Plato is a central figure in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/History_of_philosophy" title="History of philosophy">history</a> of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_philosophy" title="Ancient Greek philosophy">Ancient Greek philosophy</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Western_philosophy" title="Western philosophy">Western</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern_philosophies" title="Middle Eastern philosophies">Middle Eastern philosophies</a> descended from it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[a]</a></sup> He has also shaped <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> and <a href="/wiki/Spirituality" title="Spirituality">spirituality</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[5]</a></sup> The so-called <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">neoplatonism</a> of his interpreter <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a> greatly influenced both <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism_and_Christianity" title="Neoplatonism and Christianity">Christianity</a> (through <a href="/wiki/Church_Fathers" title="Church Fathers">Church Fathers</a> such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Augustine" title="Augustine">Augustine</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic philosophy</a> (through e.g. <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>). In modern times, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzche" title="Friedrich Nietzche">Friedrich Nietzche</a> diagnosed Western culture as growing in the shadow of Plato (famously calling Christianity "Platonism for the masses"), while <a href="/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead" title="Alfred North Whitehead">Alfred North Whitehead</a> noted: "the safest general characterization of the European philosophical tradition is that it consists of a series of <a href="/wiki/Note_(typography)" title="Note (typography)">footnotes</a> to Plato."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhitehead197839_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhitehead197839-7">[6]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato was an innovator of the written <a href="/wiki/Dialogue" title="Dialogue">dialogue</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dialectic" title="Dialectic">dialectic</a> forms in philosophy. He raised problems for what later became all the major areas of both <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_philosophy" title="Theoretical philosophy">theoretical philosophy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Practical_philosophy" title="Practical philosophy">practical philosophy</a>. His most famous contribution is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theory_of_Forms" title="Theory of Forms">theory of Forms</a> known by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pure_reason" title="Pure reason">pure reason</a>, in which Plato presents a solution to the <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_universals" title="Problem of universals">problem of universals</a>, known as <a href="/wiki/Platonism" title="Platonism">Platonism</a> (also ambiguously called either <a href="/wiki/Platonic_realism" title="Platonic realism">Platonic realism</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_idealism" title="Platonic idealism">Platonic idealism</a>). He is also the namesake of <a href="/wiki/Platonic_love" title="Platonic love">Platonic love</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonic_solids" title="Platonic solids">Platonic solids</a>.
</p><p>His own most decisive philosophical influences are usually thought to have been, along with Socrates, the <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">pre-Socratics</a> <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>, although few of his predecessors' works remain extant and much of what we know about these figures today derives from Plato himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[b]</a></sup> Unlike the work of nearly all of his contemporaries, Plato's entire body of work is believed to have survived intact for over 2,400 years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[8]</a></sup> Although their popularity has fluctuated, Plato's works have consistently been read and studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1997vii_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1997vii-11">[9]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Biography">Biography</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_life">Early life</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Early_life_of_Plato" title="Early life of Plato">Early life of Plato</a></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_and_family">Birth and family</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Diogenes_Laertius.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="517" data-file-width="399" decoding="async" height="233" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Diogenes_Laertius.jpg/180px-Diogenes_Laertius.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Diogenes_Laertius.jpg/270px-Diogenes_Laertius.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Diogenes_Laertius.jpg/360px-Diogenes_Laertius.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Diogenes_Laertius.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Diogenes Laertius is a principal source for the history of ancient Greek philosophy.</div></div></div>
<p>Little is known about Plato's early life and education. He belonged to an <a href="/wiki/Aristocracy" title="Aristocracy">aristocratic</a> and influential family. According to a disputed tradition, reported by <a href="/wiki/Doxography" title="Doxography">doxographer</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Laërtius">Diogenes Laërtius</a>, Plato's father <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ariston_(Athenian)" title="Ariston (Athenian)">Ariston</a> traced his descent from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_Athens" title="King of Athens">king of Athens</a>, <a href="/wiki/Codrus" title="Codrus">Codrus</a>, and the king of <a href="/wiki/Messenia" title="Messenia">Messenia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Melanthus" title="Melanthus">Melanthus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DW_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-DW-12">[10]</a></sup> According to the ancient Hellenic tradition, Codrus was said to have been descended from the mythological deity <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaI_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaI-14">[12]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Solon.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="584" data-file-width="350" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Solon.jpg/160px-Solon.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Solon.jpg/240px-Solon.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Solon.jpg/320px-Solon.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Solon.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Through his mother, Plato was related to Solon.</div></div></div>
<p>Plato's mother was <a href="/wiki/Perictione" title="Perictione">Perictione</a>, whose family boasted of a relationship with the famous Athenian <a href="/wiki/Legislator" title="Legislator">lawmaker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lyric_poetry" title="Lyric poetry">lyric poet</a> <a href="/wiki/Solon" title="Solon">Solon</a>, one of the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sages_of_Greece" title="Seven Sages of Greece">seven sages</a>, who repealed the laws of <a href="/wiki/Draco_(lawgiver)" title="Draco (lawgiver)">Draco</a> (except for the death penalty for <a href="/wiki/Homicide" title="Homicide">homicide</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaI_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-LaI-14">[12]</a></sup> Perictione was sister of <a href="/wiki/Charmides" title="Charmides">Charmides</a> and niece of <a href="/wiki/Critias" title="Critias">Critias</a>, both prominent figures of the <a href="/wiki/Thirty_Tyrants" title="Thirty Tyrants">Thirty Tyrants</a>, known as the Thirty, the brief <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchic</a> <a href="/wiki/Regime" title="Regime">regime</a> (404–403 BC), which followed on the collapse of Athens at the end of the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a> (431–404 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TW1_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-TW1-15">[13]</a></sup> According to some accounts, Ariston tried to force his attentions on Perictione, but failed in his purpose; then the <a href="/wiki/Twelve_Olympians" title="Twelve Olympians">god</a> <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> appeared to him in a vision, and as a result, Ariston left Perictione unmolested.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ap1_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ap1-16">[14]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exact time and place of Plato's birth are unknown. Based on ancient sources, most modern scholars believe that he was born in Athens or <a href="/wiki/Aegina" title="Aegina">Aegina</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[c]</a></sup> between 429 and 423 BC, not long after the start of the Peloponnesian War.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[d]</a></sup> The traditional date of Plato's birth during the 87th or 88th <a href="/wiki/Olympiad" title="Olympiad">Olympiad</a>, 428 or 427 BC, is based on a dubious interpretation of Diogenes Laërtius, who says, "When [Socrates] was gone, [Plato] joined <a href="/wiki/Cratylus" title="Cratylus">Cratylus</a> the Heracleitean and Hermogenes, who philosophized in the manner of Parmenides. Then, at twenty-eight, Hermodorus says, [Plato] went to <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclides in Megara</a>." However, as <a href="/wiki/Debra_Nails" title="Debra Nails">Debra Nails</a> argues, the text does not state that Plato left for Megara immediately after joining Cratylus and Hermogenes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2002247_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2002247-28">[24]</a></sup> In his <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Seventh_Letter_(Plato)" title="Seventh Letter (Plato)">Seventh Letter</a></i>, Plato notes that his coming of age coincided with the taking of power by the Thirty, remarking, "But a youth under the age of twenty made himself a laughingstock if he attempted to enter the political arena." Thus, Nails dates Plato's birth to 424/423.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails2002246_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails2002246-29">[25]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Neanthes_of_Cyzicus" title="Neanthes of Cyzicus">Neanthes</a>, Plato was six years younger than <a href="/wiki/Isocrates" title="Isocrates">Isocrates</a>, and therefore was born the same year the prominent Athenian statesman <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a> died (429 BC).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENietzsche196732_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENietzsche196732-30">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Jonathan_Barnes" title="Jonathan Barnes">Jonathan Barnes</a> regards 428 BC as the year of Plato's birth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_26-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-26">[23]</a></sup> The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Philologist" title="Philologist">grammarian</a> <a href="/wiki/Apollodorus_of_Athens" title="Apollodorus of Athens">Apollodorus of Athens</a> in his <i>Chronicles</i> argues that Plato was born in the 88th Olympiad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaII_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-LaII-22">[19]</a></sup> Both the <i><a href="/wiki/Suda" title="Suda">Suda</a></i> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Thomas_Browne" title="Sir Thomas Browne">Sir Thomas Browne</a> also claimed he was born during the 88th Olympiad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suda_20-1"><a href="#cite_note-Suda-20">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrowne1672_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrowne1672-31">[27]</a></sup> Another legend related that, when Plato was an infant, bees settled on his lips while he was sleeping: an augury of the sweetness of style in which he would discourse about philosophy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[28]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1290" data-file-width="870" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg/160px-Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg/240px-Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/85/Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg/320px-Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg 2x" width="160"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Speusippus_in_Thomas_Stanley_History_of_Philosophy.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Speusippus was Plato's nephew.</div></div></div>
<p>Besides Plato himself, Ariston and Perictione had three other children; two sons, <a href="/wiki/Adeimantus_of_Collytus" title="Adeimantus of Collytus">Adeimantus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Glaucon" title="Glaucon">Glaucon</a>, and a daughter <a href="/wiki/Potone" title="Potone">Potone</a>, the mother of <a href="/wiki/Speusippus" title="Speusippus">Speusippus</a> (the nephew and successor of Plato as head of the Academy).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TW1_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-TW1-15">[13]</a></sup> The brothers Adeimantus and Glaucon are mentioned in the <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i> as sons of Ariston,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PlRep368a_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-PlRep368a-33">[29]</a></sup> and presumably brothers of Plato, though some have argued they were uncles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[e]</a></sup> In a scenario in the <i><a href="/wiki/Memorabilia_(Xenophon)" title="Memorabilia (Xenophon)">Memorabilia</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a> confused the issue by presenting a Glaucon much younger than Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[31]</a></sup>
</p><p>Ariston appears to have died in Plato's childhood, although the precise dating of his death is difficult.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TN_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-TN-37">[32]</a></sup> Perictione then married <a href="/wiki/Pyrilampes" title="Pyrilampes">Pyrilampes</a>, her mother's brother,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NA229_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-NA229-38">[33]</a></sup> who had served many times as an ambassador to the <a href="/wiki/Achaemenid_Empire" title="Achaemenid Empire">Persian court</a> and was a friend of <a href="/wiki/Pericles" title="Pericles">Pericles</a>, the leader of the democratic faction in Athens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P13_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-P13-39">[34]</a></sup> Pyrilampes had a son from a previous marriage, Demus, who was famous for his beauty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[35]</a></sup> Perictione gave birth to Pyrilampes' second son, Antiphon, the half-brother of Plato, who appears in <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parmenides_(Plato)" title="Parmenides (Plato)">Parmenides</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-P126c_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-P126c-41">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>In contrast to his reticence about himself, Plato often introduced his distinguished relatives into his dialogues or referred to them with some precision. In addition to Adeimantus and Glaucon in the <i>Republic</i>, Charmides has a dialogue named after him; and Critias speaks in both <i><a href="/wiki/Charmides_(dialogue)" title="Charmides (dialogue)">Charmides</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Protagoras_(dialogue)" title="Protagoras (dialogue)">Protagoras</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie198611_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie198611-42">[37]</a></sup> These and other references suggest a considerable amount of family pride and enable us to reconstruct Plato's <a href="/wiki/Family_tree" title="Family tree">family tree</a>. According to Burnet, "the opening scene of the <i>Charmides</i> is a glorification of the whole [family] connection ... Plato's dialogues are not only a memorial to Socrates but also the happier days of his own family."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKahn1996186_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKahn1996186-43">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h4>
<p>The fact that the philosopher in his maturity called himself <i>Platon</i> is indisputable, but the origin of this name remains mysterious. <i>Platon</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_personal_names" title="Ancient Greek personal names">nickname</a> from the adjective <i>platýs</i> (<a class="external text" href="https://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Perseus:text:1999.04.0057:entry=platu/s" rel="nofollow"><span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">πλατύς</span></span></a>) 'broad'. Although <i>Platon</i> was a fairly common name (31 instances are known from Athens alone),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1986p._12_(footnote)_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGuthrie1986p._12_(footnote)-44">[39]</a></sup> the name does not occur in Plato's known family line.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sedley_21-2_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sedley_21-2-45">[40]</a></sup> The sources of Diogenes Laërtius account for this by claiming that his <a href="/wiki/Greek_wrestling" title="Greek wrestling">wrestling</a> coach, Ariston of Argos, dubbed him "broad" on account of his chest and shoulders, or that Plato derived his name from the breadth of his eloquence, or his wide forehead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaIV_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaIV-46">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[42]</a></sup> While recalling a <a href="/wiki/Stoicism#Ethics_and_virtues" title="Stoicism">moral lesson</a> about frugal living <a href="/wiki/Seneca_the_Younger" title="Seneca the Younger">Seneca</a> mentions the meaning of Plato's name: "His very name was given him because of his broad chest."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_(1910)_(14767984254).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1922" data-file-width="1980" decoding="async" height="214" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_%281910%29_%2814767984254%29.jpg/220px-Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_%281910%29_%2814767984254%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_%281910%29_%2814767984254%29.jpg/330px-Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_%281910%29_%2814767984254%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b6/Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_%281910%29_%2814767984254%29.jpg/440px-Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_%281910%29_%2814767984254%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Greek_athletic_sports_and_festivals_(1910)_(14767984254).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plato was a wrestler</div></div></div>
<p>His true name was supposedly <b>Aristocles</b> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Ἀριστοκλῆς</span></span>), meaning 'best reputation'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[f]</a></sup> According to Diogenes Laërtius, he was named after his grandfather, as was common in Athenian society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELaërtius1925§&amp;nbsp;4_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELaërtius1925§&amp;nbsp;4-50">[44]</a></sup> But there is only one inscription of an Aristocles, an early archon of Athens in 605/4 BC. There is no record of a line from Aristocles to Plato's father, Ariston. Recently a scholar has argued that even the name Aristocles for Plato was a much later invention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[45]</a></sup> However, another scholar claims that "there is good reason for not dismissing [the idea that Aristocles was Plato's given name] as a mere invention of his biographers", noting how prevalent that account is in our sources.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sedley_21-2_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sedley_21-2-45">[40]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span></h4>
<p>Ancient sources describe him as a bright though modest boy who excelled in his studies. <a href="/wiki/Apuleius" title="Apuleius">Apuleius</a> informs us that Speusippus praised Plato's quickness of mind and modesty as a boy, and the "first fruits of his youth infused with hard work and love of study".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ap2_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ap2-52">[46]</a></sup> His father contributed all which was necessary to give to his son a good education, and, therefore, Plato must have been instructed in <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Music" title="Music">music</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gymnastics" title="Gymnastics">gymnastics</a> by the most distinguished teachers of his time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DS_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-DS-53">[47]</a></sup> Plato invokes <a href="/wiki/Damon_of_Athens" title="Damon of Athens">Damon</a> many times in the <i>Republic</i>. Plato was a wrestler, and <a href="/wiki/Dicaearchus" title="Dicaearchus">Dicaearchus</a> went so far as to say that Plato wrestled at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Isthmian_games" title="Isthmian games">Isthmian games</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaV_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaV-54">[48]</a></sup> Plato had also attended courses of philosophy; before meeting Socrates, he first became acquainted with Cratylus and the Heraclitean doctrines.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ar987a_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ar987a-55">[49]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Ambrose" title="Ambrose">Ambrose</a> believed that Plato met <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah" title="Jeremiah">Jeremiah</a> in Egypt and was influenced by his ideas. Augustine initially accepted this claim, but later rejected it, arguing in <i><a href="/wiki/The_City_of_God" title="The City of God">The City of God</a></i> that "Plato was born a hundred years after Jeremiah prophesied."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[50]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (July 2017)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Later_life_and_death">Later life and death</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plato_i_sin_akademi,_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_(ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="688" data-file-width="830" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Plato_i_sin_akademi%2C_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_%28ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen%29.png/280px-Plato_i_sin_akademi%2C_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_%28ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Plato_i_sin_akademi%2C_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_%28ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen%29.png/420px-Plato_i_sin_akademi%2C_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_%28ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Plato_i_sin_akademi%2C_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_%28ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen%29.png/560px-Plato_i_sin_akademi%2C_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_%28ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen%29.png 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plato_i_sin_akademi,_av_Carl_Johan_Wahlbom_(ur_Svenska_Familj-Journalen).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plato in his academy, drawing after a painting by Swedish painter Carl Johan Wahlbom</div></div></div><p>Plato may have travelled in Italy, <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>, Egypt, and <a href="/wiki/Cyrene,_Libya" title="Cyrene, Libya">Cyrene</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcEvoy1984_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcEvoy1984-57">[51]</a></sup> Plato's own statement was that he visited Italy and Sicily at the age of forty and was disgusted by the sensuality of life there. Said to have returned to Athens at the age of forty, Plato founded one of the earliest known organized schools in Western Civilization on a plot of land in the Grove of Hecademus or Academus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECairns1961xiii_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECairns1961xiii-58">[52]</a></sup> This land was named after <a href="/wiki/Academus" title="Academus">Academus</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Attica" title="Attica">Attic</a> hero in <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">Greek mythology</a>. In historic Greek times it was adorned with <a href="/wiki/Platanus_orientalis" title="Platanus orientalis">oriental plane</a> and olive plantations<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[53]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[54]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Platonic_Academy" title="Platonic Academy">Academy</a> was a large enclosure of ground about six <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stadion_(unit_of_length)" title="Stadion (unit of length)">stadia</a> (a total of between a kilometer and a half mile) outside of Athens proper. One story is that the name of the Academy comes from the ancient hero, <a href="/wiki/Academus" title="Academus">Academus</a>; still another story is that the name came from a supposed former owner of the plot of land, an Athenian citizen whose name was (also) Academus; while yet another account is that it was named after a member of the army of <a href="/wiki/Castor_and_Pollux" title="Castor and Pollux">Castor and Pollux</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Regions_of_ancient_Greece#Arcadia" title="Regions of ancient Greece">Arcadian</a> named Echedemus.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERobinson182716_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson182716-61">[55]</a></sup> The Academy operated until it was destroyed by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lucius_Cornelius_Sulla" title="Lucius Cornelius Sulla">Lucius Cornelius Sulla</a> in 84 BC. Many intellectuals were schooled in the Academy, the most prominent one being Aristotle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDillon20031–3_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDillon20031–3-62">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPress20001_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPress20001-63">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>Throughout his later life, Plato became entangled with the politics of the city of <a href="/wiki/Syracuse,_Sicily" title="Syracuse, Sicily">Syracuse</a>. According to Diogenes Laërtius, Plato initially visited Syracuse while it was under the rule of <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_I_of_Syracuse" title="Dionysius I of Syracuse">Dionysius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiginos197673_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiginos197673-64">[58]</a></sup> During this first trip Dionysius's brother-in-law, <a href="/wiki/Dion_of_Syracuse" title="Dion of Syracuse">Dion of Syracuse</a>, became one of Plato's disciples, but the tyrant himself turned against Plato. Plato almost faced death, but he was sold into slavery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[g]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Anniceris" title="Anniceris">Anniceris</a>, a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cyrenaic" title="Cyrenaic">Cyrenaic</a> philosopher, subsequently bought Plato's freedom for twenty <a href="/wiki/Mina_(unit)" title="Mina (unit)">minas</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[60]</a></sup> and sent him home. After Dionysius's death, according to Plato's <i>Seventh Letter</i>, Dion requested Plato return to Syracuse to tutor <a href="/wiki/Dionysius_II_of_Syracuse" title="Dionysius II of Syracuse">Dionysius II</a> and guide him to become a philosopher king. Dionysius II seemed to accept Plato's teachings, but he became suspicious of Dion, his uncle. Dionysius expelled Dion and kept Plato against his will. Eventually Plato left Syracuse. Dion would return to overthrow Dionysius and ruled Syracuse for a short time before being usurped by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calippus_of_Syracuse" title="Calippus of Syracuse">Calippus</a>, a fellow disciple of Plato.
</p><p>According to Seneca, Plato died at the age of 81 on the same day he was born.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[61]</a></sup> The Suda indicates that he lived to 82 years,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suda_20-2"><a href="#cite_note-Suda-20">[18]</a></sup> while Neanthes claims an age of 84.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaII_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-LaII-22">[19]</a></sup> A variety of sources have given accounts of his death. One story, based on a mutilated manuscript,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiginos1976194_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiginos1976194-69">[62]</a></sup> suggests Plato died in his bed, whilst a young <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thracian" title="Thracian">Thracian</a> girl played the flute to him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchall1996_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchall1996-70">[63]</a></sup> Another tradition suggests Plato died at a wedding feast. The account is based on Diogenes Laërtius's reference to an account by Hermippus, a third-century Alexandrian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiginos1976195_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiginos1976195-71">[64]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/Tertullian" title="Tertullian">Tertullian</a>, Plato simply died in his sleep.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERiginos1976195_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERiginos1976195-71">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato owned an estate at <a href="/wiki/Iphistiadae" title="Iphistiadae">Iphistiadae</a>, which by will he left to a certain youth named Adeimantus, presumably a younger relative, as Plato had an elder brother or uncle by this name.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influences">Influences</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="599" data-file-width="449" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg/200px-Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg/300px-Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg/400px-Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kapitolinischer_Pythagoras_adjusted.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of Pythagoras in Rome.</div></div></div>
<p>Although Socrates influenced Plato directly as related in the dialogues, the influence of <a href="/wiki/Pythagoras" title="Pythagoras">Pythagoras</a> upon Plato, or in a broader sense, the <a href="/wiki/Pythagoreanism" title="Pythagoreanism">Pythagoreans</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Archytas" title="Archytas">Archytas</a> also appears to have been significant. Aristotle claimed that the philosophy of Plato closely followed the teachings of the Pythagoreans,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[65]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a> repeats this claim: "They say Plato learned all things Pythagorean."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[66]</a></sup> It is probable that both were influenced by <a href="/wiki/Orphism_(religion)" title="Orphism (religion)">Orphism</a>, and both believed in <a href="/wiki/Metempsychosis" title="Metempsychosis">metempsychosis</a>, transmigration of the <a href="/wiki/Soul" title="Soul">soul</a>.
</p><p>Pythagoras held that all things are number, and the cosmos comes from numerical principles. He introduced the concept of form as distinct from matter, and that the physical world is an imitation of an eternal mathematical world. These ideas were very influential on Heraclitus, Parmenides and Plato.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFarlane_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-McFarlane-74">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>
George Karamanolis notes that</p><blockquote><p><a href="/wiki/Numenius_of_Apamea" title="Numenius of Apamea">Numenius</a> accepted both Pythagoras and Plato as the two authorities one should follow in philosophy, but he regarded Plato's authority as subordinate to that of Pythagoras, whom he considered to be the source of all true philosophy—including Plato's own. For Numenius it is just that Plato wrote so many philosophical works, whereas Pythagoras' views were originally passed on only orally.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[68]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/R._M._Hare" title="R. M. Hare">R. M. Hare</a>, this influence consists of three points:
</p>
<ol><li>The platonic Republic might be related to the idea of "a tightly organized community of like-minded thinkers", like the one established by Pythagoras in Croton.</li>
<li>The idea that mathematics and, generally speaking, abstract thinking is a secure basis for philosophical thinking as well as "for substantial theses in <a href="/wiki/Science" title="Science">science</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morals" title="Morals">morals</a>".</li>
<li>They shared a "mystical approach to the soul and its place in the material world".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[69]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-77">[70]</a></sup></li></ol>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Plato_and_mathematics">Plato and mathematics</span></h4>
<p>Plato may have studied under the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Theodorus_of_Cyrene" title="Theodorus of Cyrene">Theodorus of Cyrene</a>, and has a <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">dialogue</a> named for and whose central character is the mathematician <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(mathematician)" title="Theaetetus (mathematician)">Theaetetus</a>. While not a mathematician, Plato was considered an accomplished teacher of mathematics. <a href="/wiki/Eudoxus_of_Cnidus" title="Eudoxus of Cnidus">Eudoxus of Cnidus</a>, the greatest mathematician in Classical Greece, who contributed much of what is found in <a href="/wiki/Euclid" title="Euclid">Euclid</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Euclid%27s_Elements" title="Euclid's Elements">Elements</a></i>, was taught by Archytas and Plato. Plato helped to distinguish between <a href="/wiki/Pure_mathematics" title="Pure mathematics">pure</a> and <a href="/wiki/Applied_mathematics" title="Applied mathematics">applied mathematics</a> by widening the gap between "arithmetic", now called <a href="/wiki/Number_theory" title="Number theory">number theory</a> and "logistic", now called <a href="/wiki/Arithmetic" title="Arithmetic">arithmetic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[h]</a></sup>
</p>

title="Mysterium Cosmographicum">Mysterium Cosmographicum</a></i></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:170px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg"><img alt="A platonic solid as a part of Spinoza monument in Amsterdam." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3861" data-file-width="3861" decoding="async" height="168" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg/168px-Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg/252px-Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg/336px-Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg 2x" width="168"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Icosahedron-spinoza.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A detail of <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">Spinoza</a> monument in <a href="/wiki/Amsterdam" title="Amsterdam">Amsterdam</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In the dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i> Plato associated each of the four <a href="/wiki/Classical_element" title="Classical element">classical elements</a> (<a href="/wiki/Earth_(classical_element)" title="Earth (classical element)">earth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Air_(classical_element)" title="Air (classical element)">air</a>, <a href="/wiki/Water_(classical_element)" title="Water (classical element)">water</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Fire_(classical_element)" title="Fire (classical element)">fire</a>) with a regular solid (<a href="/wiki/Cube" title="Cube">cube</a>, <a href="/wiki/Octahedron" title="Octahedron">octahedron</a>, <a href="/wiki/Icosahedron" title="Icosahedron">icosahedron</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Tetrahedron" title="Tetrahedron">tetrahedron</a> respectively) due to their shape, the so-called Platonic solids. The fifth regular solid, the <a href="/wiki/Dodecahedron" title="Dodecahedron">dodecahedron</a>, was supposed to be the element which made up the heavens.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Heraclitus_and_Parmenides">Heraclitus and Parmenides</span></h3>
<p>The two philosophers <a href="/wiki/Heraclitus" title="Heraclitus">Heraclitus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a>, following the way initiated by <a href="/wiki/Pre-Socratic_philosophy" title="Pre-Socratic philosophy">pre-Socratic Greek philosophers</a> such as Pythagoras, depart from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mythology" title="Mythology">mythology</a> and begin the <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a> tradition that strongly influenced Plato and continues today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFarlane_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-McFarlane-74">[67]</a></sup>
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1096954695/mw-parser-output/.tmulti" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="thumb tmulti tright"><div class="thumbinner multiimageinner" style="width:396px;max-width:396px"><div class="trow"><div class="tsingle" style="width:202px;max-width:202px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1303" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg/200px-Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg/300px-Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/30/Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg/400px-Hendrik_ter_Brugghen_-_Heraclitus.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption"><i>Heraclitus</i> (1628) by <a href="/wiki/Hendrick_ter_Brugghen" title="Hendrick ter Brugghen">Hendrick ter Brugghen</a></div></div><div class="tsingle" style="width:190px;max-width:190px"><div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Parmenides.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="370" data-file-width="283" decoding="async" height="246" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Parmenides.jpg/188px-Parmenides.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ed/Parmenides.jpg/282px-Parmenides.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ed/Parmenides.jpg 2x" width="188"/></a></div><div class="thumbcaption">Bust of Parmenides from <a href="/wiki/Velia" title="Velia">Velia</a></div></div></div></div></div>
<p>The surviving fragments written by Heraclitus suggest the view that all things are <a href="/wiki/Impermanence" title="Impermanence">continuously changing</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Becoming_(philosophy)" title="Becoming (philosophy)">becoming</a>. His image of the river, with ever-changing waters, is well known. According to some ancient traditions such as that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes Laërtius">Diogenes Laërtius</a>, Plato received these ideas through Heraclitus' disciple <a href="/wiki/Cratylus" title="Cratylus">Cratylus</a>, who held the more radical view that continuous change warrants <a href="/wiki/Philosophical_skepticism" title="Philosophical skepticism">scepticism</a> because we cannot define a thing that does not have a permanent nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Large_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-Large-80">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Parmenides adopted an altogether contrary vision, arguing for the idea of changeless <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">Being</a> and the view that change is an illusion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-McFarlane_74-2"><a href="#cite_note-McFarlane-74">[67]</a></sup> John Palmer notes "Parmenides' distinction among the principal modes of being and his derivation of the attributes that must belong to what must be, simply as such, qualify him to be seen as the founder of metaphysics or <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontology</a> as a domain of inquiry distinct from <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">theology</a>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Palmer_81-0"><a href="#cite_note-Palmer-81">[73]</a></sup>
</p><p>These ideas about change and permanence, or becoming and Being, influenced Plato in formulating his theory of Forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Large_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-Large-80">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato's most self-critical dialogue is the <i>Parmenides</i>, which features Parmenides and his student <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Elea" title="Zeno of Elea">Zeno</a>, who, following Parmenides' denial of change, argued forcefully through his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeno%27s_Paradoxes" title="Zeno's Paradoxes">paradoxes</a> to deny the existence of <a href="/wiki/Motion" title="Motion">motion</a>.
</p><p>Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Sophist_(dialogue)" title="Sophist (dialogue)">Sophist</a></i> dialogue includes an <a href="/wiki/Eleatics" title="Eleatics">Eleatic</a> stranger, a follower of Parmenides, as a foil for his arguments against Parmenides. In the dialogue, Plato distinguishes <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a> and <a href="/wiki/Verb" title="Verb">verbs</a>, providing some of the earliest treatment of <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a> and <a href="/wiki/Predicate_(grammar)" title="Predicate (grammar)">predicate</a>. He also argues that motion and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_(physics)" title="Rest (physics)">rest</a> both "are", against followers of Parmenides who say rest is but motion is not.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Socrates">Socrates</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Socratic_problem" title="Socratic problem">Socratic problem</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_Louvre.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2592" data-file-width="1944" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Socrates_Louvre.jpg/200px-Socrates_Louvre.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Socrates_Louvre.jpg/300px-Socrates_Louvre.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Socrates_Louvre.jpg/400px-Socrates_Louvre.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Socrates_Louvre.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust of <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Louvre" title="Louvre">Louvre</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Plato was one of the devoted young followers of <a href="/wiki/Socrates" title="Socrates">Socrates</a>. The precise relationship between Plato and Socrates remains an area of contention among scholars.
</p><p>Plato never speaks in his own voice in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socratic_dialogues" title="Socratic dialogues">his dialogues</a>; every dialogue except the <i><a href="/wiki/Laws_(dialogue)" title="Laws (dialogue)">Laws</a></i> features Socrates, although many dialogues, including the <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Statesman_(dialogue)" title="Statesman (dialogue)">Statesman</a></i>, feature him speaking only rarely. In the <a href="/wiki/Second_Letter_(Plato)" title="Second Letter (Plato)"><i>Second Letter</i></a>, it says, "no writing of Plato exists or ever will exist, but those now said to be his are those of a Socrates become beautiful and new";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[74]</a></sup> if the letter is Plato's, the final qualification seems to call into question the dialogues' historical fidelity. In any case, <a href="/wiki/Xenophon" title="Xenophon">Xenophon</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Memorabilia_(Xenophon)" title="Memorabilia (Xenophon)">Memorabilia</a></i> and <a href="/wiki/Aristophanes" title="Aristophanes">Aristophanes</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Clouds" title="The Clouds">The Clouds</a></i> seem to present a somewhat different portrait of Socrates from the one Plato paints. The <a href="/wiki/Socratic_problem" title="Socratic problem">Socratic problem</a> concerns how to reconcile these various accounts. <a href="/wiki/Leo_Strauss" title="Leo Strauss">Leo Strauss</a> notes that Socrates' reputation for <a href="/wiki/Irony" title="Irony">irony</a> casts doubt on whether Plato's Socrates is expressing sincere beliefs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEStrauss196450–51_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrauss196450–51-83">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>Aristotle attributes a different doctrine with respect to Forms to Plato and Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[76]</a></sup> Aristotle suggests that Socrates' idea of forms can be discovered through investigation of the natural world, unlike Plato's Forms that exist beyond and outside the ordinary range of human understanding. In the dialogues of Plato though, Socrates sometimes seems to support a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mystical" title="Mystical">mystical</a> side, discussing <a href="/wiki/Reincarnation" title="Reincarnation">reincarnation</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Roman_mysteries" title="Greco-Roman mysteries">mystery religions</a>, this is generally attributed to Plato.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (August 2020)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[77]</a></sup> Regardless, this view of Socrates cannot be dismissed out of hand, as we cannot be sure of the differences between the views of Plato and Socrates. In the <i><a href="/wiki/Meno" title="Meno">Meno</a>,</i> Plato refers to the <a href="/wiki/Eleusinian_Mysteries" title="Eleusinian Mysteries">Eleusinian Mysteries</a>, telling Meno he would understand Socrates's answers better if he could stay for the initiations next week. It is possible that Plato and Socrates took part in the Eleusinian Mysteries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metaphysics">Metaphysics</span></h3>
<p>In Plato's dialogues, Socrates and his company of disputants had something to say on many subjects, including several aspects of <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysics</a>. These include religion and science, human nature, love, and sexuality. More than one dialogue contrasts perception and <a href="/wiki/Reality" title="Reality">reality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">nature</a> and custom, and body and soul. <a href="/wiki/F._M._Cornford" title="F. M. Cornford">Francis Cornford</a> referred to the "twin pillars of Platonism" as being the theory of Forms on the one hand, and, on the other hand, the doctrine of immortality of the soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[79]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Forms">The Forms</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Theory_of_forms" title="Theory of forms">Plato's theory of Forms</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:132px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="540" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg/130px-Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg/195px-Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/26/Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg/260px-Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg.png 2x" width="130"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Illustration_to_Euclid%27s_proof_of_the_Pythagorean_theorem.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The "windmill proof" of the Pythagorean theorem found in Euclid's <i>Elements</i>.</div></div></div>
<p>"Platonism" and its theory of Forms (or theory of Ideas) denies the reality of the material world, considering it only an image or copy of the real world. The theory of Forms is first introduced in the <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedo" title="Phaedo">Phaedo</a></i> dialogue (also known as <i>On the Soul</i>), wherein Socrates refutes the <a href="/wiki/Pluralism_(philosophy)" title="Pluralism (philosophy)">pluralism</a> of the likes of <a href="/wiki/Anaxagoras" title="Anaxagoras">Anaxagoras</a>, then the most popular response to Heraclitus and Parmenides, while giving the "Opposites Argument" in support of the Forms.
</p><p>According to this theory of Forms, there are at least two worlds: the apparent world of <a href="/wiki/Abstract_and_concrete" title="Abstract and concrete">concrete</a> objects, grasped by the <a href="/wiki/Sense" title="Sense">senses</a>, which constantly changes, and an unchanging and unseen world of Forms or <a href="/wiki/Abstract_object_theory" title="Abstract object theory">abstract objects</a>, grasped by <a href="/wiki/Speculative_reason" title="Speculative reason">pure reason</a> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">λογική</span></span>), which ground what is apparent.
</p><p>It can also be said there are three worlds, with the apparent world consisting of both the world of material objects and of mental images, with the "third realm" consisting of the Forms. Thus, though there is the term "Platonic idealism", this refers to Platonic Ideas or the Forms, and not to some platonic kind of <a href="/wiki/Idealism" title="Idealism">idealism</a>, an 18th-century view which sees <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">matter</a> as unreal in favour of <a href="/wiki/Mind" title="Mind">mind</a>. For Plato, though grasped by the mind, only the Forms are truly real.
</p><p>Plato's Forms thus represent <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Type_(metaphysics)" title="Type (metaphysics)">types</a> of things, as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Property_(metaphysics)" title="Property (metaphysics)">properties</a>, patterns, and <a href="/wiki/Relations_(philosophy)" title="Relations (philosophy)">relations</a>, to which we refer as objects. Just as individual tables, chairs, and cars refer to objects in this world, 'tableness', 'chairness', and 'carness', as well as e. g. <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Truth" title="Truth">truth</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Beauty" title="Beauty">beauty</a> refer to objects in another world. One of Plato's most cited examples for the Forms were the truths of <a href="/wiki/Geometry" title="Geometry">geometry</a>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Pythagorean_theorem" title="Pythagorean theorem">Pythagorean theorem</a>.
</p><p>In other words, the Forms are <a href="/wiki/Universal_(metaphysics)" title="Universal (metaphysics)">universals</a> given as a solution to the problem of universals, or the problem of "the One and the Many", e. g. how one predicate "red" can apply to many red objects. For Plato, this is because there is one abstract object or Form of red, redness itself, in which the several red things "participate". As Plato's solution is that universals are Forms and that Forms are real if anything is, Plato's philosophy is unambiguously called Platonic realism. According to Aristotle, Plato's best-known argument in support of the Forms was the "one over many" argument.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[80]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Temida,_Gdansk_Court.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3888" decoding="async" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Temida%2C_Gdansk_Court.jpg/200px-Temida%2C_Gdansk_Court.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Temida%2C_Gdansk_Court.jpg/300px-Temida%2C_Gdansk_Court.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Temida%2C_Gdansk_Court.jpg/400px-Temida%2C_Gdansk_Court.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Temida,_Gdansk_Court.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>What is justice?</i></div></div></div>
<p>Aside from being immutable, timeless, changeless, and one over many, the Forms also provide definitions and the standard against which all instances are measured. In the dialogues Socrates regularly asks for the meaning – in the sense of <a href="/wiki/Intension" title="Intension">intensional</a> <a href="/wiki/Definition" title="Definition">definitions</a> – of a general term (e. g. justice, truth, beauty), and criticizes those who instead give him particular, <a href="/wiki/Extension_(semantics)" title="Extension (semantics)">extensional</a> examples, rather than the quality shared by all examples.
</p><p>There is thus a world of perfect, eternal, and changeless meanings of predicates, the Forms, existing in the <a href="/wiki/Hyperuranion" title="Hyperuranion">realm</a> of Being outside of <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">space and time</a>; and the imperfect sensible world of becoming, subjects somehow in <a href="/wiki/Kh%C3%B4ra" title="Khôra">a state between being and nothing</a>, that partakes of the qualities of the Forms, and is its instantiation.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_soul">The soul</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_theory_of_soul" title="Plato's theory of soul">Plato's theory of soul</a></div>
<p>For Plato, as was characteristic of ancient Greek philosophy, the soul was that which gave life. See this brief exchange from the <i>Phaedo</i>: "What is it that, when present in a body, makes it living? — A soul."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another hallmark of Plato's view of the soul is that it is what rules and controls a person's body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[82]</a></sup> Plato uses this observation in the <i>Alcibiades</i> as evidence that people are their souls.
</p><p>Plato advocates a belief in the immortality of the soul, and several dialogues end with long speeches imagining the <a href="/wiki/Afterlife" title="Afterlife">afterlife</a>. In the <i>Timaeus</i>, Socrates locates the parts of the soul within the human body: Reason is located in the head, spirit in the top third of the <a href="/wiki/Torso" title="Torso">torso</a>, and the appetite in the middle third of the torso, down to the <a href="/wiki/Navel" title="Navel">navel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[83]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorter2006360_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorter2006360-92">[84]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, Plato evinces in multiple dialogues (such as the <i>Phaedo</i> and <i>Timaeus</i>) a belief in the theory of reincarnation. Scholars debate whether he intends the theory to be literally true, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[85]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Epistemology">Epistemology</span></h3>
<p>Plato also discusses several aspects of <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemology</a>. More than one dialogue contrasts knowledge (<i><a href="/wiki/Episteme" title="Episteme">episteme</a></i>) and opinion (<i><a href="/wiki/Doxa" title="Doxa">doxa</a></i>). <a href="/wiki/Platonic_epistemology" title="Platonic epistemology">Plato's epistemology</a> involves Socrates (and other characters, such as Timaeus) arguing that knowledge is not <a href="/wiki/Empiricism" title="Empiricism">empirical</a>, and that it comes from divine insight. The Forms are also responsible for both knowledge or certainty, and are grasped by pure reason.
</p><p>In several dialogues, Socrates inverts the common man's intuition about what is knowable and what is real. Reality is unavailable to those who use their senses. Socrates says that he who sees with his eyes is blind. While most people take the objects of their senses to be real if anything is, Socrates is contemptuous of people who think that something has to be graspable in the hands to be real. In the <i>Theaetetus</i>, he says such people are <i>eu amousoi</i> (εὖ ἄμουσοι), an expression that means literally, "happily without the muses".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[86]</a></sup> In other words, such people are willingly ignorant, living without divine inspiration and access to higher insights about reality.
</p><p>In Plato's dialogues, Socrates always insists on his ignorance and humility, that <a href="/wiki/I_know_that_I_know_nothing" title="I know that I know nothing">he knows nothing</a>, so-called "<a href="/wiki/Irony#Socratic_irony" title="Irony">Socratic irony</a>." Several dialogues refute a series of viewpoints, but offer no positive position, thus ending in <i><a href="/wiki/Aporia" title="Aporia">aporia</a></i>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Recollection">Recollection</span></h4>
<p>In several of Plato's dialogues, Socrates promulgates the idea that knowledge is a matter of <a href="/wiki/Anamnesis_(philosophy)" title="Anamnesis (philosophy)">recollection</a> of things acquainted with before one is born, and not of observation or study.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBaird_&amp;_Kaufmann2008_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBaird_&amp;_Kaufmann2008-95">[87]</a></sup> Keeping with the theme of admitting his own ignorance, Socrates regularly complains of his forgetfulness. In the <i>Meno</i>, Socrates uses a geometrical example to expound Plato's view that knowledge in this latter sense is acquired by recollection. Socrates elicits a fact concerning a geometrical construction from a slave boy, who could not have otherwise known the fact (due to the slave boy's lack of education). The knowledge must be present, Socrates concludes, in an eternal, non-experiential form.
</p><p>In other dialogues, the <i>Sophist</i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Statesman_(dialogue)" title="Statesman (dialogue)">Statesman</a></i>, <i>Republic</i>, and the <i>Parmenides</i>, Plato himself associates knowledge with the apprehension of unchanging Forms and their relationships to one another (which he calls "expertise" in dialectic), including through the processes of <i>collection</i> and <i>division</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2011176–187_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2011176–187-96">[88]</a></sup> More explicitly, Plato himself argues in the <i>Timaeus</i> that knowledge is always proportionate to the realm from which it is gained. In other words, if one derives one's account of something experientially, because the world of sense is in flux, the views therein attained will be mere opinions. Meanwhile, opinions are characterized by a lack of necessity and stability. On the other hand, if one derives one's account of something by way of the non-sensible forms, because these forms are unchanging, so too is the account derived from them. That apprehension of forms is required for knowledge may be taken to cohere with Plato's theory in the <i>Theaetetus</i> and <i>Meno</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELee2011432_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELee2011432-97">[89]</a></sup> Indeed, the apprehension of Forms may be at the base of the "account" required for justification, in that it offers <a href="/wiki/Foundationalism" title="Foundationalism">foundational</a> knowledge which itself needs no account, thereby avoiding an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Infinite_regression" title="Infinite regression">infinite regression</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylor2011189_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylor2011189-98">[90]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Justified_true_belief">Justified true belief</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="342" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/220px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/330px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/83/Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg/440px-Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Classical_definition_of_Kno.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Venn diagram illustrating the classical theory of knowledge.</div></div></div>
<p>Many have interpreted Plato as stating — even having been the first to write — that <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Justified_true_belief" title="Justified true belief">justified true belief</a>, an influential view that informed future developments in epistemology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine20035_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine20035-99">[91]</a></sup> This interpretation is partly based on a reading of the <i>Theaetetus</i> wherein Plato argues that knowledge is distinguished from mere true belief by the knower having an "account" of the object of their true belief.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[92]</a></sup> And this theory may again be seen in the <i>Meno</i>, where it is suggested that true belief can be raised to the level of knowledge if it is bound with an account as to the question of "why" the object of the true belief is so.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[93]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDowell1973230_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDowell1973230-102">[94]</a></sup>
</p><p>Many years later, <a href="/wiki/Edmund_Gettier" title="Edmund Gettier">Edmund Gettier</a> famously demonstrated the <a href="/wiki/Gettier_problem" title="Gettier problem">problems</a> of the justified true belief account of knowledge. That the modern theory of justified true belief as knowledge, which Gettier addresses, is equivalent to Plato's is accepted by some scholars but rejected by others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1979366_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1979366-103">[95]</a></sup> Plato himself also identified problems with the <i>justified true belief</i> definition in the <i>Theaetetus</i>, concluding that justification (or an "account") would require knowledge of <i>difference</i>, meaning that the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Definition_of_knowledge" title="Definition of knowledge">definition of knowledge</a> is <a href="/wiki/Circular_reasoning" title="Circular reasoning">circular</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[96]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcDowell1973256_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcDowell1973256-105">[97]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ethics">Ethics</span></h3>
<p>Several dialogues discuss <a href="/wiki/Ethics" title="Ethics">ethics</a> including virtue and vice, pleasure and pain, crime and punishment, and justice and medicine. Plato views "The Good" as the supreme Form, somehow existing even "beyond being".
</p><p>Socrates propounded a <a href="/wiki/Moral_intellectualism" title="Moral intellectualism">moral intellectualism</a> which claimed nobody does bad on purpose, and to know what is good results in doing what is good; that knowledge is virtue. In the <i>Protagoras</i> dialogue it is argued that virtue is innate and cannot be learned.
</p><p>Socrates presents the famous <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro_dilemma" title="Euthyphro dilemma">Euthyphro dilemma</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro" title="Euthyphro">dialogue</a> of the same name: "Is the <a href="/wiki/Piety" title="Piety">pious</a> (<a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BD%85%CF%83%CE%B9%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:ὅσιος">τὸ ὅσιον</a>) loved by the <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">gods</a> because it is pious, or is it pious because it is loved by the gods?" (<a href="/wiki/Stephanus_pagination" title="Stephanus pagination">10a</a>)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Justice">Justice</span></h4>
<p>As above, in the <i>Republic</i>, Plato asks the question, “What is justice?” By means of the Greek term <i>dikaiosune</i> – a term for “justice” that captures both individual justice and the justice that informs societies, Plato is able not only to inform metaphysics, but also ethics and politics with the question: “What is the basis of moral and social obligation?” Plato's well-known answer rests upon the fundamental responsibility to seek wisdom, wisdom which leads to an understanding of the Form of the Good. Plato further argues that such understanding of Forms produces and ensures the good communal life when ideally structured under a philosopher king in a society with three classes (philosopher kings, guardians, and workers) that neatly mirror his triadic view of the individual soul (reason, spirit, and appetite). In this manner, justice is obtained when knowledge of how to fulfill one's moral and political function in society is put into practice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[98]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics">Politics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="970" data-file-width="920" decoding="async" height="232" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg/220px-P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg/330px-P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3d/P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg/440px-P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:P._Oxy._LII_3679.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus_Papyri" title="Oxyrhynchus Papyri">Oxyrhynchus Papyri</a>, with fragment of Plato's <i>Republic</i></div></div></div>
<p>The dialogues also discuss politics. Some of Plato's most famous doctrines are contained in the <i>Republic</i> as well as in the <i><a href="/wiki/Laws_(dialogue)" title="Laws (dialogue)">Laws</a></i> and the <i>Statesman</i>. Because these opinions are not spoken directly by Plato and vary between dialogues, they cannot be straightforwardly assumed as representing Plato's own views.
</p><p>Socrates asserts that societies have a tripartite class structure corresponding to the appetite/spirit/reason structure of the individual soul. The appetite/spirit/reason are analogous to the castes of society.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlössner2007345–349_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlössner2007345–349-107">[99]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><i>Productive</i> (Workers) – the labourers, carpenters, plumbers, masons, merchants, farmers, ranchers, etc. These correspond to the "appetite" part of the soul.</li>
<li><i>Protective</i> (Warriors or Guardians) – those who are adventurous, strong and brave; in the armed forces. These correspond to the "spirit" part of the soul.</li>
<li><i>Governing</i> (Rulers or Philosopher Kings) – those who are intelligent, rational, self-controlled, in love with wisdom, well suited to make decisions for the community. These correspond to the "reason" part of the soul and are very few.</li></ul>
<p>According to this model, the principles of <a href="/wiki/Athenian_democracy" title="Athenian democracy">Athenian democracy</a> (as it existed in his day) are rejected as only a few are fit to rule. Instead of rhetoric and persuasion, Socrates says reason and wisdom should govern. As Socrates puts it:
</p>
<dl><dd>"Until philosophers rule as kings or those who are now called kings and leading men genuinely and adequately philosophize, that is, until political power and philosophy entirely coincide, while the many natures who at present pursue either one exclusively are forcibly prevented from doing so, cities will have no rest from evils,... nor, I think, will the human race."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[100]</a></sup></dd></dl>
<p>Socrates describes these "philosopher kings" as "those who love the sight of truth"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[101]</a></sup> and supports the idea with the analogy of a captain and his ship or a doctor and his medicine. According to him, sailing and health are not things that everyone is qualified to practice by nature.
