A large part of the <i>Republic</i> then addresses how the educational system should be set up to produce these philosopher kings.
</p><p>In addition, the ideal city is used as an image to illuminate the state of one's soul, or the <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">will</a>, <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">reason</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Interpersonal_attraction" title="Interpersonal attraction">desires</a> combined in the human body. Socrates is attempting to make an image of a rightly ordered human, and then later goes on to describe the different kinds of humans that can be observed, from tyrants to lovers of money in various kinds of cities. The ideal city is not promoted, but only used to magnify the different kinds of individual humans and the state of their soul. However, the philosopher king image was used by many after Plato to justify their personal political beliefs. The philosophic soul according to Socrates has reason, will, and desires united in virtuous harmony. A philosopher has the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moderate" title="Moderate">moderate</a> love for <a href="/wiki/Wisdom" title="Wisdom">wisdom</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Courage" title="Courage">courage</a> to act according to wisdom. Wisdom is <a href="/wiki/Knowledge" title="Knowledge">knowledge</a> about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Goodness_and_value_theory" title="Goodness and value theory">Good</a> or the right relations between all that <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">exists</a>.
</p><p>Wherein it concerns states and rulers, Socrates asks which is better—a bad democracy or a country reigned by a tyrant. He argues that it is better to be ruled by a bad tyrant, than by a bad democracy (since here all the people are now responsible for such actions, rather than one individual committing many bad deeds.) This is emphasised within the <i>Republic</i> as Socrates describes the event of mutiny on board a ship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[102]</a></sup> Socrates suggests the ship's crew to be in line with the democratic rule of many and the captain, although inhibited through ailments, the tyrant. Socrates' description of this event is parallel to that of democracy within the state and the inherent problems that arise.
</p><p>According to Socrates, a state made up of different kinds of souls will, overall, decline from an aristocracy (rule by the best) to a <a href="/wiki/Timocracy" title="Timocracy">timocracy</a> (rule by the honourable), then to an oligarchy (rule by the few), then to a <a href="/wiki/Democracy" title="Democracy">democracy</a> (rule by the people), and finally to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tyranny" title="Tyranny">tyranny</a> (rule by one person, rule by a tyrant).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlössner2007350_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlössner2007350-111">[103]</a></sup> Aristocracy in the sense of government (politeia) is advocated in Plato's Republic. This regime is ruled by a <a href="/wiki/Philosopher_king" title="Philosopher king">philosopher king</a>, and thus is grounded on wisdom and reason.
</p><p>The aristocratic state, and the man whose nature corresponds to it, are the objects of Plato's analyses throughout much of the <i>Republic</i>, as opposed to the other four types of states/men, who are discussed later in his work. In Book VIII, Socrates states in order the other four imperfect societies with a description of the state's structure and individual character. In timocracy, the ruling class is made up primarily of those with a warrior-like character.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[104]</a></sup> Oligarchy is made up of a society in which wealth is the criterion of merit and the wealthy are in control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[105]</a></sup> In democracy, the state bears resemblance to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" title="Ancient Athens">ancient Athens</a> with traits such as equality of political opportunity and freedom for the individual to do as he likes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[106]</a></sup> Democracy then degenerates into tyranny from the conflict of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rich_and_poor" title="Rich and poor">rich and poor</a>. It is characterized by an undisciplined society existing in chaos, where the tyrant rises as a popular champion leading to the formation of his private army and the growth of oppression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlössner2007350_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlössner2007350-111">[103]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDorter2006253–267_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDorter2006253–267-116">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_poetry">Art and poetry</span></h3>
<p>Several dialogues tackle questions about art, including rhetoric and rhapsody. Socrates says that poetry is inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Muses" title="Muses">muses</a>, and is not rational. He speaks approvingly of this, and other forms of divine madness (drunkenness, eroticism, and dreaming) in the <i>Phaedrus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[109]</a></sup> and yet in the <i>Republic</i> wants to outlaw Homer's great poetry, and laughter as well. In <i><a href="/wiki/Ion_(dialogue)" title="Ion (dialogue)">Ion</a></i>, Socrates gives no hint of the disapproval of Homer that he expresses in the <i>Republic</i>. The dialogue <i>Ion</i> suggests that Homer's <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> functioned in the ancient Greek world as the Bible does today in the modern Christian world: as divinely inspired literature that can provide moral guidance, if only it can be properly interpreted.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Rhetoric">Rhetoric</span></h3>
<p>Scholars often view Plato's philosophy as at odds with rhetoric due to his criticisms of rhetoric in the <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgias_(dialogue)" title="Gorgias (dialogue)">Gorgias</a></i> and his ambivalence toward rhetoric expressed in the <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a></i>. But other contemporary researchers contest the idea that Plato despised rhetoric and instead view his dialogues as a dramatization of complex rhetorical principles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[111]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Unwritten_doctrines">Unwritten doctrines</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_unwritten_doctrines" title="Plato's unwritten doctrines">Plato's unwritten doctrines</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="746" data-file-width="548" decoding="async" height="299" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg/220px-Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg/330px-Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg/440px-Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Dionysus-or-Plato-Herculaneum-papyri-Villa-of-the-Papyri-Barker.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Bust excavated at the <a href="/wiki/Villa_of_the_Papyri" title="Villa of the Papyri">Villa of the Papyri</a>, possibly of <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, Plato or <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>.</div></div></div><p>For a long time, <a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_unwritten_doctrines" title="Plato's unwritten doctrines">Plato's unwritten doctrines</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERodriguez-Grandjean1998_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERodriguez-Grandjean1998-121">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[115]</a></sup> had been controversial. Many modern books on Plato seem to diminish its importance; nevertheless, the first important witness who mentions its existence is Aristotle, who in his <i><a href="/wiki/Physics_(Aristotle)" title="Physics (Aristotle)">Physics</a></i> writes: "It is true, indeed, that the account he gives there [i.e. in <i>Timaeus</i>] of the participant is different from what he says in his so-called <i>unwritten teachings</i> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_language" title="Ancient Greek language">Ancient Greek</a>: <span lang="grc">ἄγραφα δόγματα</span>, <small><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Ancient_Greek" title="Romanization of Ancient Greek">romanized</a>: </small><span title="Ancient Greek-language romanization"><i lang="grc-Latn"><span lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek transliteration"><i>agrapha dogmata</i></span></i></span>)."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[116]</a></sup> The term "<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἄγραφα <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">δόγματα</a></span></span>" literally means <i>unwritten doctrines</i> or <i>unwritten <a href="/wiki/Dogma" title="Dogma">dogmas</a></i> and it stands for the most fundamental metaphysical teaching of Plato, which he disclosed only orally, and some say only to his most trusted fellows, and which he may have kept secret from the public. The importance of the unwritten doctrines does not seem to have been seriously questioned before the 19th century.
</p><p>A reason for not revealing it to everyone is partially discussed in <i>Phaedrus</i> where Plato criticizes the written transmission of knowledge as faulty, favouring instead the spoken <i><a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos">logos</a></i>: "he who has knowledge of the just and the good and beautiful ... will not, when in earnest, write them in ink, sowing them through a pen with words, which cannot defend themselves by argument and cannot teach the truth effectually."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[117]</a></sup> The same argument is repeated in Plato's <i>Seventh Letter</i>: "every serious man in dealing with really serious subjects carefully avoids writing."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[118]</a></sup> In the same letter he writes: "I can certainly declare concerning all these writers who claim to know the subjects that I seriously study ... there does not exist, nor will there ever exist, any treatise of mine dealing therewith."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[119]</a></sup> Such secrecy is necessary in order not "to expose them to unseemly and degrading treatment".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is, however, said that Plato once disclosed this knowledge to the public in his lecture <i>On the Good</i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Περὶ τἀγαθοῦ</span></span>), in which the Good (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὸ ἀγαθόν</span></span>) is identified with the One (the Unity, <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὸ ἕν</span></span>), the fundamental ontological principle. The content of this lecture has been transmitted by several witnesses. <a href="/wiki/Aristoxenus" title="Aristoxenus">Aristoxenus</a> describes the event in the following words: "Each came expecting to learn something about the things that are generally considered good for men, such as wealth, good health, physical strength, and altogether a kind of wonderful happiness. But when the mathematical demonstrations came, including numbers, geometrical figures and astronomy, and finally the statement Good is One seemed to them, I imagine, utterly unexpected and strange; hence some belittled the matter, while others rejected it."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[121]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Simplicius_of_Cilicia" title="Simplicius of Cilicia">Simplicius</a> quotes <a href="/wiki/Alexander_of_Aphrodisias" title="Alexander of Aphrodisias">Alexander of Aphrodisias</a>, who states that "according to Plato, the first principles of everything, including the Forms themselves are One and Indefinite Duality (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἡ ἀόριστος δυάς</span></span>), which he called Large and Small (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὸ μέγα καὶ τὸ μικρόν</span></span>)", and Simplicius reports as well that "one might also learn this from Speusippus and Xenocrates and the others who were present at Plato's lecture on the Good".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-51">[45]</a></sup>
</p><p>Their account is in full agreement with Aristotle's description of Plato's metaphysical doctrine. In <i><a href="/wiki/Metaphysics_(Aristotle)" title="Metaphysics (Aristotle)">Metaphysics</a></i> he writes: "Now since the Forms are the causes of everything else, he [i.e. Plato] supposed that their elements are the elements of all things. Accordingly, the material principle is the Great and Small [i.e. the Dyad], and the essence is the One (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὸ ἕν</span></span>), since the numbers are derived from the Great and Small by participation in the One".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_130-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-130">[122]</a></sup> "From this account it is clear that he only employed two causes: that of the essence, and the material cause; for the Forms are the cause of the essence in everything else, and the One is the cause of it in the Forms. He also tells us what the material substrate is of which the Forms are predicated in the case of sensible things, and the One in that of the Forms—that it is this the duality (the Dyad, <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἡ δυάς</span></span>), the Great and Small (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὸ μέγα καὶ τὸ μικρόν</span></span>). Further, he assigned to these two elements respectively the causation of good and of evil".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto1_130-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto1-130">[122]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important aspect of this interpretation of Plato's metaphysics is the continuity between his teaching and the Neoplatonic interpretation of <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[i]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ficino" title="Ficino">Ficino</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[j]</a></sup> which has been considered erroneous by many but may in fact have been directly influenced by oral transmission of Plato's doctrine. A modern scholar who recognized the importance of the unwritten doctrine of Plato was <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_Gomperz" title="Heinrich Gomperz">Heinrich Gomperz</a> who described it in his speech during the 7th <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_Congress_of_Philosophy" title="International Congress of Philosophy">International Congress of Philosophy</a> in 1930.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGomperz1931_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGomperz1931-133">[123]</a></sup> All the sources related to the <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ἄγραφα δόγματα</span></span> have been collected by Konrad Gaiser and published as <i>Testimonia Platonica</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaiser1998_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaiser1998-134">[124]</a></sup> These sources have subsequently been interpreted by scholars from the German <i>Tübingen School of interpretation</i> such as Hans Joachim Krämer or Thomas A. Szlezák.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[k]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Themes_of_Plato.27s_dialogues"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Themes_of_Plato's_dialogues">Themes of Plato's dialogues</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Trial_of_Socrates">Trial of Socrates</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2559" data-file-width="3896" decoding="async" height="217" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg/330px-David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg/495px-David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg/660px-David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:David_-_The_Death_of_Socrates.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i><a href="/wiki/The_Death_of_Socrates" title="The Death of Socrates">The Death of Socrates</a></i> (1787), by <a href="/wiki/Jacques-Louis_David" title="Jacques-Louis David">Jacques-Louis David</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Trial_of_Socrates" title="Trial of Socrates">trial of Socrates</a> and his death sentence is the central, unifying event of Plato's dialogues. It is relayed in the dialogues <a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)"><i>Apology</i></a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crito" title="Crito">Crito</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedo" title="Phaedo">Phaedo</a></i>. <i>Apology</i> is Socrates' defence speech, and <i>Crito</i> and <i>Phaedo</i> take place in prison after the conviction.
</p><p><i>Apology</i> is among the most frequently read of Plato's works. In the <i>Apology</i>, Socrates tries to dismiss rumours that he is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sophism" title="Sophism">sophist</a> and defends himself against charges of disbelief in the gods and corruption of the young. Socrates insists that long-standing slander will be the real cause of his demise, and says the legal charges are essentially false. Socrates famously denies being wise, and explains how his life as a philosopher was launched by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oracle_at_Delphi" title="Oracle at Delphi">Oracle at Delphi</a>. He says that his quest to resolve the riddle of the oracle put him at odds with his fellow man, and that this is the reason he has been mistaken for a menace to the city-state of Athens.
</p><p>In <i>Apology</i>, Socrates is presented as mentioning Plato by name as one of those youths close enough to him to have been corrupted, if he were in fact guilty of corrupting the youth, and questioning why their fathers and brothers did not step forward to testify against him if he was indeed guilty of such a crime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[125]</a></sup> Later, Plato is mentioned along with Crito, Critobolus, and Apollodorus as offering to pay a fine of 30 <a href="/wiki/Mina_(unit)" title="Mina (unit)">minas</a> on Socrates' behalf, in lieu of the death penalty proposed by <a href="/wiki/Meletus" title="Meletus">Meletus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[126]</a></sup> In the <i>Phaedo</i>, the title character lists those who were in attendance at the prison on Socrates' last day, explaining Plato's absence by saying, "Plato was ill".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[127]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_trial_in_other_dialogues">The trial in other dialogues</span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_speakers_in_Plato%27s_dialogues" title="List of speakers in Plato's dialogues">List of speakers in Plato's dialogues</a></div>
<p>If Plato's important dialogues do not refer to Socrates' execution explicitly, they allude to it, or use characters or themes that play a part in it. Five dialogues foreshadow the trial: In the <i>Theaetetus</i> and the <i>Euthyphro</i> Socrates tells people that he is about to face corruption charges.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-140"><a href="#cite_note-140">[129]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Meno" title="Meno">Meno</a></i>, one of the men who brings legal charges against Socrates, <a href="/wiki/Anytus" title="Anytus">Anytus</a>, warns him about the trouble he may get into if he does not stop criticizing important people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[130]</a></sup> In the <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgias" title="Gorgias">Gorgias</a></i>, Socrates says that his trial will be like a doctor prosecuted by a cook who asks a jury of children to choose between the doctor's bitter medicine and the cook's tasty treats.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[131]</a></sup> In the <i>Republic</i>, Socrates explains why an enlightened man (presumably himself) will stumble in a courtroom situation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[132]</a></sup> Plato's support of aristocracy and distrust of democracy is also taken to be partly rooted in a democracy having killed Socrates. In the <i>Protagoras</i>, Socrates is a guest at the home of <a href="/wiki/Callias_III" title="Callias III">Callias</a>, son of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hipponicus" title="Hipponicus">Hipponicus</a>, a man whom Socrates disparages in the <i>Apology</i> as having wasted a great amount of money on sophists' fees.
</p><p>Two other important dialogues, the <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i> and the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(Plato)" title="Phaedrus (Plato)">Phaedrus</a></i>, are linked to the main storyline by characters. In the <i>Apology</i>, Socrates says Aristophanes slandered him in a comic play, and blames him for causing his bad reputation, and ultimately, his death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[133]</a></sup> In the <i>Symposium</i>, the two of them are drinking together with other friends. The character Phaedrus is linked to the main story line by character (Phaedrus is also a participant in the <i>Symposium</i> and the <i>Protagoras</i>) and by theme (the philosopher as divine emissary, etc.) The <i>Protagoras</i> is also strongly linked to the <i>Symposium</i> by characters: all of the formal speakers at the <i>Symposium</i> (with the exception of Aristophanes) are present at the home of Callias in that dialogue. Charmides and his guardian Critias are present for the discussion in the <i>Protagoras</i>. Examples of characters crossing between dialogues can be further multiplied. The <i>Protagoras</i> contains the largest gathering of Socratic associates.
</p><p>In the dialogues Plato is most celebrated and admired for, Socrates is concerned with human and political virtue, has a distinctive personality, and friends and enemies who "travel" with him from dialogue to dialogue. This is not to say that Socrates is consistent: a man who is his friend in one dialogue may be an adversary or subject of his mockery in another. For example, Socrates praises the wisdom of <a href="/wiki/Euthyphro_(prophet)" title="Euthyphro (prophet)">Euthyphro</a> many times in the <i><a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)">Cratylus</a></i>, but makes him look like a fool in the <i>Euthyphro</i>. He disparages sophists generally, and <a href="/wiki/Prodicus" title="Prodicus">Prodicus</a> specifically in the <i>Apology</i>, whom he also slyly jabs in the <i>Cratylus</i> for charging the hefty fee of fifty <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Drachma" title="Drachma">drachmas</a> for a course on language and grammar. However, Socrates tells Theaetetus in his namesake dialogue that he admires Prodicus and has directed many pupils to him. Socrates' ideas are also not consistent within or between or among dialogues.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Allegories">Allegories</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Allegorical_interpretations_of_Plato" title="Allegorical interpretations of Plato">Allegorical interpretations of Plato</a></div>
<p><i>Mythos</i> and <i>logos</i> are terms that evolved throughout classical Greek history. In the times of <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a> (i.e., the 8th century BC) they were essentially synonyms, and contained the meaning of 'tale' or 'history'. Later came historians like Herodotus and Thucydides, as well as philosophers like Heraclitus and Parmenides and other Presocratics who introduced a distinction between both terms; mythos became more a <i>nonverifiable account</i>, and logos a <i>rational account</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[134]</a></sup> It may seem that Plato, being a disciple of Socrates and a strong partisan of philosophy based on <i>logos</i>, should have avoided the use of myth-telling. Instead, he made abundant use of it. This fact has produced analytical and interpretative work, in order to clarify the reasons and purposes for that use. Furthermore, Plato himself often oscillates between calling one and the same thing a <i>muthos</i> and a <i>logos</i>, revealing a preference for the earlier view that the two terms are synonyms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[135]</a></sup>
</p><p>Plato, in general, distinguished between three types of myth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[l]</a></sup> First, there were the false myths, like those based on stories of gods subject to passions and sufferings, because reason teaches that God is perfect. Then came the myths based on true reasoning, and therefore also true. Finally, there were those non-verifiable because beyond of human reason, but containing some truth in them. Regarding the subjects of Plato's myths, they are of two types, those dealing with the origin of the universe, and those about morals and the origin and fate of the soul.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[136]</a></sup>
</p><p>It is generally agreed that the main purpose for Plato in using myths was didactic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[137]</a></sup> He considered that only a few people were capable or interested in following a reasoned philosophical discourse, but men in general are attracted by stories and tales. Consequently, then, he used the myth to convey the conclusions of the philosophical reasoning. Some of Plato's myths were based in traditional ones, others were modifications of them, and finally, he also invented altogether new myths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[138]</a></sup> Notable examples include the story of <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Myth_of_Er" title="Myth of Er">Myth of Er</a>, and the Allegory of the Cave.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Cave">The Cave</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1166" data-file-width="1600" decoding="async" height="240" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg/330px-Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg/495px-Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg/660px-Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Platon_Cave_Sanraedam_1604.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plato's Allegory of the Cave by <a href="/wiki/Jan_Saenredam" title="Jan Saenredam">Jan Saenredam</a>, according to <a href="/wiki/Cornelis_van_Haarlem" title="Cornelis van Haarlem">Cornelis van Haarlem</a>, 1604, <a href="/wiki/Albertina" title="Albertina">Albertina</a>, Vienna</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allegory_of_the_Cave" title="Allegory of the Cave">Allegory of the Cave</a></div>
<p>The theory of Forms is most famously captured in his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Allegory_of_the_Cave" title="Allegory of the Cave">Allegory of the Cave</a>, and more explicitly in his <a href="/wiki/Analogy_of_the_sun" title="Analogy of the sun">analogy of the sun</a> and <a href="/wiki/Analogy_of_the_divided_line" title="Analogy of the divided line">the divided line</a>. The Allegory of the Cave is a paradoxical analogy wherein Socrates argues that the invisible world is the most intelligible (<i>noeton</i>) and that the visible world (<i>(h)oraton</i>) is the least knowable, and the most obscure.
</p><p>Socrates says in the <i>Republic</i> that people who take the sun-lit world of the senses to be good and real are living pitifully in a den of evil and ignorance. Socrates admits that few climb out of the den, or cave of ignorance, and those who do, not only have a terrible struggle to attain the heights, but when they go back down for a visit or to help other people up, they find themselves objects of scorn and ridicule.
</p><p>According to Socrates, physical objects and physical events are "shadows" of their ideal or perfect forms, and exist only to the extent that they instantiate the perfect versions of themselves. Just as shadows are temporary, inconsequential epiphenomena produced by physical objects, physical objects are themselves fleeting phenomena caused by more substantial causes, the ideals of which they are mere instances. For example, Socrates thinks that perfect justice exists (although it is not clear where) and his own trial would be a cheap copy of it.
</p><p>The Allegory of the Cave is intimately connected to his political ideology, that only people who have climbed out of the cave and cast their eyes on a vision of goodness are fit to rule. Socrates claims that the enlightened men of society must be forced from their divine contemplation and be compelled to run the city according to their lofty insights. Thus is born the idea of the "<a href="/wiki/Philosopher_king" title="Philosopher king">philosopher-king</a>", the wise person who accepts the power thrust upon him by the people who are wise enough to choose a good master. This is the main thesis of Socrates in the <i>Republic</i>, that the most wisdom the masses can muster is the wise choice of a ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[139]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ring_of_Gyges">Ring of Gyges</span></h4>
<p>A ring which could make one invisible, the <a href="/wiki/Ring_of_Gyges" title="Ring of Gyges">Ring of Gyges</a> is proposed in the <i>Republic</i> by the character of Glaucon, and considered by the rest of the characters for its ethical consequences, whether an individual possessing it would be most happy abstaining or doing injustice.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Chariot">Chariot</span></h4>
<p>He also compares the soul (<a href="/wiki/Psyche_(psychology)" title="Psyche (psychology)"><i>psyche</i></a>) to a <a href="/wiki/Chariot_Allegory" title="Chariot Allegory">chariot</a>. In this allegory he introduces a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plato%27s_tripartite_theory_of_soul" title="Plato's tripartite theory of soul">triple soul</a> composed of a charioteer and two horses. The charioteer is a symbol of the intellectual and logical part of the soul (<a href="/wiki/Logos" title="Logos"><i>logistikon</i></a>), and the two horses represent the moral virtues (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thymos" title="Thymos"><i>thymoeides</i></a>) and passionate instincts (<i><a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Epithymetikon&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Epithymetikon (page does not exist)">epithymetikon</a></i>), respectively, to illustrate the conflict between them.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialectic">Dialectic</span></h3>
<p>Socrates employs a <a href="/wiki/Socratic_method" title="Socratic method">dialectic method</a> which proceeds by <a href="/wiki/Socratic_questioning" title="Socratic questioning">questioning</a>. The role of dialectic in Plato's thought is contested but there are two main interpretations: a type of reasoning and a method of intuition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackburn1996104_152-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackburn1996104-152">[140]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Simon_Blackburn" title="Simon Blackburn">Simon Blackburn</a> adopts the first, saying that Plato's dialectic is "the process of eliciting the truth by means of questions aimed at opening out what is already implicitly known, or at exposing the contradictions and muddles of an opponent's position."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlackburn1996104_152-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlackburn1996104-152">[140]</a></sup> A similar interpretation has been put forth by Louis Hartz, who compares Plato's dialectic to that of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hegel" title="Hegel">Hegel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[141]</a></sup> According to this view, opposing arguments improve upon each other, and prevailing opinion is shaped by the synthesis of many conflicting ideas over time. Each new idea exposes a flaw in the accepted model, and the epistemological substance of the debate continually approaches the truth. Hartz's is a teleological interpretation at the core, in which philosophers will ultimately exhaust the available body of knowledge and thus reach "the end of history." <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a>, on the other hand, claims that dialectic is the art of intuition for "visualising the divine originals, the Forms or Ideas, of unveiling the Great Mystery behind the common man's everyday world of appearances."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPopper1962133_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPopper1962133-154">[142]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h3>
<p>Plato often discusses the father-son relationship and the question of whether a father's interest in his sons has much to do with how well his sons turn out. In ancient Athens, a boy was socially located by his family identity, and Plato often refers to his characters in terms of their paternal and fraternal relationships. Socrates was not a family man, and saw himself as the son of his mother, who was apparently a <a href="/wiki/Midwife" title="Midwife">midwife</a>. A divine <a href="/wiki/Fatalism" title="Fatalism">fatalist</a>, Socrates mocks men who spent exorbitant fees on tutors and trainers for their sons, and repeatedly ventures the idea that good character is a gift from the gods. Plato's dialogue <i>Crito</i> reminds Socrates that <a href="/wiki/Orphan" title="Orphan">orphans</a> are at the mercy of chance, but Socrates is unconcerned. In the <i>Theaetetus</i>, he is found recruiting as a disciple a young man whose <a href="/wiki/Inheritance" title="Inheritance">inheritance</a> has been squandered. Socrates twice compares the relationship of the older man and his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Athenian_pederasty" title="Athenian pederasty">boy lover</a> to the father-son relationship,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-155"><a href="#cite_note-155">[143]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-156"><a href="#cite_note-156">[144]</a></sup> and in the <i>Phaedo</i>, Socrates' disciples, towards whom he displays more concern than his biological sons, say they will feel "fatherless" when he is gone.
</p><p>Though Plato <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plato%27s_views_on_women" title="Plato's views on women">agreed with Aristotle that women were inferior to men</a>, in the fourth book of the <i>Republic</i> the character of Socrates says this was only because of <i>nomos</i> or custom and not because of nature, and thus women needed <i>paidia</i>, rearing or education to be equal to men. In the "merely probable tale" of the eponymous character in the <i>Timaeus</i>, unjust men who live corrupted lives would be reincarnated as women or various animal kinds.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Narration">Narration</span></h3>
<p>Plato never presents himself as a participant in any of the dialogues, and with the exception of the <i>Apology</i>, there is no suggestion that he heard any of the dialogues firsthand. Some dialogues have no narrator but have a pure "dramatic" form (examples: <i>Meno</i>, <i>Gorgias</i>, <i>Phaedrus</i>, <i>Crito</i>, <i>Euthyphro</i>), some dialogues are narrated by Socrates, wherein he speaks in first person (examples: <i><a href="/wiki/Lysis_(dialogue)" title="Lysis (dialogue)">Lysis</a></i>, <i>Charmides</i>, <i>Republic</i>). One dialogue, <i>Protagoras</i>, begins in dramatic form but quickly proceeds to Socrates' narration of a conversation he had previously with the sophist for whom the dialogue is named; this narration continues uninterrupted till the dialogue's end.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_(zweite_Fassung)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1692" data-file-width="3613" decoding="async" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_%28zweite_Fassung%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/400px-Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_%28zweite_Fassung%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_%28zweite_Fassung%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/600px-Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_%28zweite_Fassung%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_%28zweite_Fassung%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg/800px-Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_%28zweite_Fassung%29_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anselm_Feuerbach_-_Das_Gastmahl._Nach_Platon_(zweite_Fassung)_-_Google_Art_Project.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Painting of a scene from Plato's <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Anselm_Feuerbach" title="Anselm Feuerbach">Anselm Feuerbach</a>, 1873)</div></div></div>
<p>Two dialogues <i>Phaedo</i> and <i>Symposium</i> also begin in dramatic form but then proceed to virtually uninterrupted narration by followers of Socrates. <i>Phaedo</i>, an account of Socrates' final conversation and hemlock drinking, is narrated by Phaedo to Echecrates in a foreign city not long after the execution took place.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-158"><a href="#cite_note-158">[m]</a></sup> The <i>Symposium</i> is narrated by Apollodorus, a Socratic disciple, apparently to Glaucon. Apollodorus assures his listener that he is recounting the story, which took place when he himself was an infant, not from his own memory, but as remembered by Aristodemus, who told him the story years ago.
</p><p>The <i>Theaetetus</i> is a peculiar case: a dialogue in dramatic form embedded within another dialogue in dramatic form. In the beginning of the <i>Theaetetus</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-159"><a href="#cite_note-159">[146]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Euclid_of_Megara" title="Euclid of Megara">Euclides</a> says that he compiled the conversation from notes he took based on what Socrates told him of his conversation with the title character. The rest of the <i>Theaetetus</i> is presented as a "book" written in dramatic form and read by one of Euclides' slaves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[147]</a></sup> Some scholars take this as an indication that Plato had by this date wearied of the narrated form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnet1928a§&amp;nbsp;177_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnet1928a§&amp;nbsp;177-161">[148]</a></sup> With the exception of the <i>Theaetetus</i>, Plato gives no explicit indication as to how these orally transmitted conversations came to be written down.
</p>
<h2><span id="History_of_Plato.27s_dialogues"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="History_of_Plato's_dialogues">History of Plato's dialogues</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1588" data-file-width="2023" decoding="async" height="275" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg/350px-Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg/525px-Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg/700px-Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Timaeus_stephanus_pages_32_33.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Volume 3, pp. 32–33, of the 1578 Stephanus edition of Plato, showing a passage of <i>Timaeus</i> with the Latin translation and notes of <a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Serres" title="Jean de Serres">Jean de Serres</a></div></div></div>
<p>Thirty-five dialogues and thirteen letters (the <a href="/wiki/Epistles_(Plato)" title="Epistles (Plato)"><i>Epistles</i></a>) have traditionally been ascribed to Plato, though modern scholarship doubts the authenticity of at least some of these. Plato's writings have been published in several fashions; this has led to several conventions regarding the naming and referencing of Plato's texts.
</p><p>The usual system for making unique references to sections of the text by Plato derives from a 16th-century edition of Plato's works by <a href="/wiki/Henri_Estienne" title="Henri Estienne">Henricus Stephanus</a> known as <a href="/wiki/Stephanus_pagination" title="Stephanus pagination">Stephanus pagination</a>.
</p><p>One tradition regarding the arrangement of Plato's texts is according to <a href="/wiki/Tetralogy" title="Tetralogy">tetralogies</a>. This scheme is ascribed by Diogenes Laërtius to an ancient scholar and court astrologer to <a href="/wiki/Tiberius" title="Tiberius">Tiberius</a> named <a href="/wiki/Thrasyllus_of_Mendes" title="Thrasyllus of Mendes">Thrasyllus</a>. The list includes works of doubtful authenticity (written in italic), and includes the Letters.
</p>
<ul><li>1st tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Euthyphro" title="Euthyphro">Euthyphro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)">Apology</a>, <a href="/wiki/Crito" title="Crito">Crito</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaedo" title="Phaedo">Phaedo</a></li></ul></li>
<li>2nd tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)">Cratylus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theatetus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sophist_(dialogue)" title="Sophist (dialogue)">Sophist</a>, <a href="/wiki/Statesman_(dialogue)" title="Statesman (dialogue)">Statesman</a></li></ul></li>
<li>3nd tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Parmenides_(dialogue)" title="Parmenides (dialogue)">Parmenides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philebus" title="Philebus">Philebus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a>, <a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a></li></ul></li>
<li>4th tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/First_Alcibiades" title="First Alcibiades">Alcibiades I</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Alcibiades" title="Second Alcibiades">Alcibiades II</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hipparchus_(dialogue)" title="Hipparchus (dialogue)">Hipparchus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Rival_Lovers" title="Rival Lovers">Lovers</a></i></li></ul></li>
<li>5th tetralogy
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Theages" title="Theages">Theages</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Charmides_(dialogue)" title="Charmides (dialogue)">Charmides</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laches_(dialogue)" title="Laches (dialogue)">Laches</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lysis_(dialogue)" title="Lysis (dialogue)">Lysis</a></li></ul></li>
<li>6th tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_(dialogue)" title="Euthydemus (dialogue)">Euthydemus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protagoras_(dialogue)" title="Protagoras (dialogue)">Protagoras</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gorgias_(dialogue)" title="Gorgias (dialogue)">Gorgias</a>, <a href="/wiki/Meno" title="Meno">Meno</a></li></ul></li>
<li>7th tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Hippias_Major" title="Hippias Major">Hippias Major</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hippias_Minor" title="Hippias Minor">Hippias Minor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ion_(dialogue)" title="Ion (dialogue)">Ion</a>, <a href="/wiki/Menexenus_(dialogue)" title="Menexenus (dialogue)">Menexenus</a></li></ul></li>
<li>8th tetralogy
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Clitophon_(dialogue)" title="Clitophon (dialogue)">Clitophon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Critias_(dialogue)" title="Critias (dialogue)">Critias</a></li></ul></li>
<li>9th tetralogy
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Minos_(dialogue)" title="Minos (dialogue)">Minos</a></i>, <a href="/wiki/Laws_(dialogue)" title="Laws (dialogue)">Laws</a>, <i><a href="/wiki/Epinomis" title="Epinomis">Epinomis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Epistles_(Plato)" title="Epistles (Plato)">Letters</a></i></li></ul></li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Chronology">Chronology</span></h3>
<p>No one knows the exact order Plato's dialogues were written in, nor the extent to which some might have been later revised and rewritten. The works are usually grouped into <i>Early</i> (sometimes by some into <i>Transitional</i>), <i>Middle</i>, and <i>Late</i> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-162"><a href="#cite_note-162">[149]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[150]</a></sup> This choice to group chronologically is thought worthy of criticism by some (Cooper <i>et al</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[151]</a></sup> given that it is recognized that there is no absolute agreement as to the true chronology, since the facts of the temporal order of writing are not confidently ascertained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-165"><a href="#cite_note-165">[152]</a></sup> Chronology was not a consideration in ancient times, in that groupings of this nature are <i>virtually absent</i> (Tarrant) in the extant writings of ancient Platonists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-166"><a href="#cite_note-166">[153]</a></sup>
</p><p>Whereas those classified as "early dialogues" often conclude in aporia, the so-called "middle dialogues" provide more clearly stated positive teachings that are often ascribed to Plato such as the theory of Forms. The remaining dialogues are classified as "late" and are generally agreed to be difficult and challenging pieces of philosophy. This grouping is the only one proven by stylometric analysis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1997xiv_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1997xiv-167">[154]</a></sup> Among those who classify the dialogues into periods of composition, Socrates figures in all of the "early dialogues" and they are considered the most faithful representations of the historical Socrates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds2004_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds2004-168">[155]</a></sup>
</p><p>The following represents one relatively common division.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-169"><a href="#cite_note-169">[156]</a></sup> It should, however, be kept in mind that many of the positions in the ordering are still highly disputed, and also that the very notion that Plato's dialogues can or should be "ordered" is by no means universally accepted. Increasingly in the most recent Plato scholarship, writers are sceptical of the notion that the order of Plato's writings can be established with any precision,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[157]</a></sup> though Plato's works are still often characterized as falling at least roughly into three groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>Early: <i><a href="/wiki/Apology_(Plato)" title="Apology (Plato)">Apology</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Charmides_(dialogue)" title="Charmides (dialogue)">Charmides</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Crito" title="Crito">Crito</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Euthyphro" title="Euthyphro">Euthyphro</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Gorgias_(dialogue)" title="Gorgias (dialogue)">Gorgias</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hippias_Minor" title="Hippias Minor">Hippias Minor</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Hippias_Major" title="Hippias Major">Hippias Major</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Ion_(dialogue)" title="Ion (dialogue)">Ion</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Laches_(dialogue)" title="Laches (dialogue)">Laches</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Lysis_(dialogue)" title="Lysis (dialogue)">Lysis</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Protagoras_(dialogue)" title="Protagoras (dialogue)">Protagoras</a></i>
</p><p>Middle: <i><a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)">Cratylus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Euthydemus_(dialogue)" title="Euthydemus (dialogue)">Euthydemus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Meno" title="Meno">Meno</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Parmenides_(dialogue)" title="Parmenides (dialogue)">Parmenides</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedo" title="Phaedo">Phaedo</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Phaedrus_(dialogue)" title="Phaedrus (dialogue)">Phaedrus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Theaetetus_(dialogue)" title="Theaetetus (dialogue)">Theatetus</a></i>
</p><p>Late: <i><a href="/wiki/Critias_(dialogue)" title="Critias (dialogue)">Critias</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sophist_(dialogue)" title="Sophist (dialogue)">Sophist</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Statesman_(dialogue)" title="Statesman (dialogue)">Statesman</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Philebus" title="Philebus">Philebus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Laws_(dialogue)" title="Laws (dialogue)">Laws</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds2004_168-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds2004-168">[155]</a></sup></i>
</p><p>A significant distinction of the early Plato and the later Plato has been offered by scholars such as E.R. Dodds and has been summarized by Harold Bloom in his book titled <i>Agon</i>: "E.R. Dodds is the classical scholar whose writings most illuminated the Hellenic descent (in) <i>The Greeks and the Irrational</i> ... In his chapter on Plato and the Irrational Soul ... Dodds traces Plato's spiritual evolution from the pure rationalist of the <i>Protagoras</i> to the transcendental psychologist, influenced by the Pythagoreans and Orphics, of the later works culminating in the <i>Laws</i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBloom19825_171-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBloom19825-171">[158]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Lewis_Campbell_(classicist)" title="Lewis Campbell (classicist)">Lewis Campbell</a> was the first<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBurnet1928b9_172-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBurnet1928b9-172">[159]</a></sup> to make exhaustive use of <a href="/wiki/Stylometry" title="Stylometry">stylometry</a> to prove the great probability that the <i>Critias</i>, <i>Timaeus</i>, <i>Laws</i>, <i>Philebus</i>, <i>Sophist</i>, and <i>Statesman</i> were all clustered together as a group, while the <i>Parmenides</i>, <i>Phaedrus</i>, <i>Republic</i>, and <i>Theaetetus</i> belong to a separate group, which must be earlier (given Aristotle's statement in his <i>Politics</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-173"><a href="#cite_note-173">[160]</a></sup> that the <i>Laws</i> was written after the <i>Republic</i>; cf. Diogenes Laërtius <i>Lives</i> 3.37). What is remarkable about Campbell's conclusions is that, in spite of all the stylometric studies that have been conducted since his time, perhaps the only chronological fact about Plato's works that can now be said to be <i>proven</i> by stylometry is the fact that <i>Critias</i>, <i>Timaeus</i>, <i>Laws</i>, <i>Philebus</i>, <i>Sophist</i>, and <i>Statesman</i> are the latest of Plato's dialogues, the others earlier.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1997xiv_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1997xiv-167">[154]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Protagoras</i> is often considered one of the last of the "early dialogues". Three dialogues are often considered "transitional" or "pre-middle": <i>Euthydemus</i>, <i>Gorgias</i>, and <i>Meno</i>. Proponents of dividing the dialogues into periods often consider the <i>Parmenides</i> and <i>Theaetetus</i> to come late in the middle period and be transitional to the next, as they seem to treat the theory of Forms critically (<i>Parmenides</i>) or only indirectly (<i>Theaetetus</i>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandwood1990251_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandwood1990251-174">[161]</a></sup> Ritter's stylometric analysis places <i>Phaedrus</i> as probably after <i>Theaetetus</i> and <i>Parmenides</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandwood199077_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandwood199077-175">[162]</a></sup> although it does not relate to the theory of Forms in the same way. The first book of the <i>Republic</i> is often thought to have been written significantly earlier than the rest of the work, although possibly having undergone revisions when the later books were attached to it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrandwood1990251_174-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrandwood1990251-174">[161]</a></sup>
</p><p>While looked to for Plato's "mature" answers to the questions posed by his earlier works, those answers are difficult to discern. Some scholars<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds2004_168-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds2004-168">[155]</a></sup> indicate that the theory of Forms is absent from the late dialogues, its having been refuted in the <i>Parmenides</i>, but there is not total consensus that the <i>Parmenides</i> actually refutes the theory of Forms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMeinwald1991_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMeinwald1991-176">[163]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Writings_of_doubted_authenticity">Writings of doubted authenticity</span></h4>
<p>Jowett mentions in his Appendix to Menexenus, that works which bore the character of a writer were attributed to that writer even when the actual author was unknown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-177"><a href="#cite_note-177">[164]</a></sup>
</p><p>For below:
</p><p>(*) if there is no consensus among scholars as to whether Plato is the author, and (‡) if most scholars agree that Plato is <i>not</i> the author of the work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-178"><a href="#cite_note-178">[165]</a></sup>
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/First_Alcibiades" title="First Alcibiades">Alcibiades I</a></i> (*), <i><a href="/wiki/Second_Alcibiades" title="Second Alcibiades">Alcibiades II</a></i> (‡), <i><a href="/wiki/Clitophon_(dialogue)" title="Clitophon (dialogue)">Clitophon</a></i> (*), <i><a href="/wiki/Epinomis" title="Epinomis">Epinomis</a></i> (‡), <i><a href="/wiki/Epistles_(Plato)" title="Epistles (Plato)">Letters</a></i> (*), <i><a href="/wiki/Hipparchus_(dialogue)" title="Hipparchus (dialogue)">Hipparchus</a></i> (‡), <i><a href="/wiki/Menexenus_(dialogue)" title="Menexenus (dialogue)">Menexenus</a></i> (*), <i><a href="/wiki/Minos_(dialogue)" title="Minos (dialogue)">Minos</a></i> (‡), <i><a href="/wiki/Rival_Lovers" title="Rival Lovers">Lovers</a></i> (‡), <i><a href="/wiki/Theages" title="Theages">Theages</a></i> (‡)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Spurious_writings">Spurious writings</span></h4>
<p>The following works were transmitted under Plato's name, most of them already considered spurious in antiquity, and so were not included by Thrasyllus in his tetralogical arrangement. These works are labelled as <i>Notheuomenoi</i> ("spurious") or <i>Apocrypha</i>.
</p><p><i><a href="/wiki/Axiochus_(dialogue)" title="Axiochus (dialogue)">Axiochus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Definitions_(Plato)" title="Definitions (Plato)">Definitions</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Demodocus_(dialogue)" title="Demodocus (dialogue)">Demodocus</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Epigrams_(Plato)" title="Epigrams (Plato)">Epigrams</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Eryxias_(dialogue)" title="Eryxias (dialogue)">Eryxias</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Halcyon_(dialogue)" title="Halcyon (dialogue)">Halcyon</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_Justice" title="On Justice">On Justice</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/On_Virtue" title="On Virtue">On Virtue</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Sisyphus_(dialogue)" title="Sisyphus (dialogue)">Sisyphus</a></i>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Textual_sources_and_history">Textual sources and history</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_manuscripts_of_Plato%27s_dialogues" title="List of manuscripts of Plato's dialogues">List of manuscripts of Plato's dialogues</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1536" data-file-width="1021" decoding="async" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg/220px-Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg/330px-Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg/440px-Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Clarke_Plato_page_1_recto.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First page of the <i>Euthyphro</i>, from the Clarke Plato (Codex Oxoniensis Clarkianus 39), 895 AD. The text is <a href="/wiki/Greek_minuscule" title="Greek minuscule">Greek minuscule</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Some 250 known manuscripts of Plato survive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrumbaughWells1989_179-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrumbaughWells1989-179">[166]</a></sup> The texts of Plato as received today apparently represent the complete written philosophical work of Plato and are generally good by the standards of <a href="/wiki/Textual_criticism" title="Textual criticism">textual criticism</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[167]</a></sup> No modern edition of Plato in the original Greek represents a single source, but rather it is reconstructed from multiple sources which are compared with each other. These sources are medieval manuscripts written on vellum (mainly from 9th to 13th century AD Byzantium), papyri (mainly from late antiquity in Egypt), and from the independent <i>testimonia</i> of other authors who quote various segments of the works (which come from a variety of sources). The text as presented is usually not much different from what appears in the Byzantine manuscripts, and papyri and testimonia just confirm the manuscript tradition. In some editions, however, the readings in the papyri or testimonia are favoured in some places by the editing critic of the text. Reviewing editions of papyri for the <i>Republic</i> in 1987, Slings suggests that the use of papyri is hampered due to some poor editing practices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlings198731_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlings198731-181">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the first century AD, <a href="/wiki/Thrasyllus_of_Mendes" title="Thrasyllus of Mendes">Thrasyllus of Mendes</a> had compiled and published the works of Plato in the original Greek, both genuine and spurious. While it has not survived to the present day, all the extant medieval Greek manuscripts are based on his edition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1997viii–xii_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1997viii–xii-182">[169]</a></sup>
</p><p>The oldest surviving complete manuscript for many of the dialogues is the Clarke Plato (Codex Oxoniensis Clarkianus 39, or Codex Boleianus MS E.D. Clarke 39), which was written in Constantinople in 895 and acquired by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford University</a> in 1809.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-183"><a href="#cite_note-183">[170]</a></sup> The Clarke is given the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sigla" title="Sigla">siglum</a> <i>B</i> in modern editions. <i>B</i> contains the first six tetralogies and is described internally as being written by "John the Calligrapher" on behalf of <a href="/wiki/Arethas_of_Caesarea" title="Arethas of Caesarea">Arethas of Caesarea</a>. It appears to have undergone corrections by Arethas himself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds195935–36_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds195935–36-184">[171]</a></sup> For the last two tetralogies and the apocrypha, the oldest surviving complete manuscript is Codex Parisinus graecus 1807, designated <i>A</i>, which was written nearly contemporaneously to <i>B</i>, circa 900 AD.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds195937_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds195937-185">[172]</a></sup> <i>A</i> must be a copy of the edition edited by the <a href="/wiki/Patriarch" title="Patriarch">patriarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Photios_I_of_Constantinople" title="Photios I of Constantinople">Photios</a>, teacher of Arethas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-186"><a href="#cite_note-186">[173]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-187"><a href="#cite_note-187">[174]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[175]</a></sup><i>A</i> probably had an initial volume containing the first 7 tetralogies which is now lost, but of which a copy was made, Codex Venetus append. class. 4, 1, which has the siglum <i>T</i>. The oldest manuscript for the seventh tetralogy is Codex Vindobonensis 54. suppl. phil. Gr. 7, with siglum <i>W</i>, with a supposed date in the twelfth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds195939_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds195939-189">[176]</a></sup> In total there are fifty-one such Byzantine manuscripts known, while others may yet be found.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrwin201171_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrwin201171-190">[177]</a></sup>
</p><p>To help establish the text, the older evidence of papyri and the independent evidence of the testimony of commentators and other authors (i.e., those who quote and refer to an old text of Plato which is no longer extant) are also used. Many papyri which contain fragments of Plato's texts are among the <a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus_Papyri" title="Oxyrhynchus Papyri">Oxyrhynchus Papyri</a>. The 2003 <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Classical_Texts" title="Oxford Classical Texts">Oxford Classical Texts</a> edition by Slings even cites the Coptic translation of a fragment of the <i>Republic</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Nag_Hammadi_library" title="Nag Hammadi library">Nag Hammadi library</a> as evidence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESlings2003xxiii_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESlings2003xxiii-191">[178]</a></sup> Important authors for testimony include <a href="/wiki/Olympiodorus_the_Younger" title="Olympiodorus the Younger">Olympiodorus the Younger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Plutarch" title="Plutarch">Plutarch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Iamblichus" title="Iamblichus">Iamblichus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eusebius" title="Eusebius">Eusebius</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Stobaeus" title="Stobaeus">Stobaeus</a>.
</p><p>During the early Renaissance, the Greek language and, along with it, Plato's texts were reintroduced to Western Europe by Byzantine scholars. In September or October 1484 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Filippo_Valori" title="Filippo Valori">Filippo Valori</a> and <a href="/wiki/Francesco_Berlinghieri" title="Francesco Berlinghieri">Francesco Berlinghieri</a> printed 1025 copies of <a href="/wiki/Marsilio_Ficino" title="Marsilio Ficino">Ficino's</a> translation, using the printing press at the Dominican convent S.Jacopo di Ripoli.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-192"><a href="#cite_note-192">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAllen197512_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAllen197512-193">[180]</a></sup> Cosimo had been influenced toward studying Plato by the many Byzantine Platonists in Florence during his day, including <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Gemistus_Plethon" title="George Gemistus Plethon">George Gemistus Plethon</a>.
</p><p>The 1578 edition<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-194"><a href="#cite_note-194">[181]</a></sup> of Plato's complete works published by Henricus Stephanus (<a href="/wiki/Henri_Estienne" title="Henri Estienne">Henri Estienne</a>) in <a href="/wiki/Geneva" title="Geneva">Geneva</a> also included parallel Latin translation and running commentary by Joannes Serranus (<a href="/wiki/Jean_de_Serres" title="Jean de Serres">Jean de Serres</a>). It was this edition which established standard <a href="/wiki/Stephanus_pagination" title="Stephanus pagination">Stephanus pagination</a>, still in use today.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESuzanne2009_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESuzanne2009-195">[182]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern_editions">Modern editions</span></h4>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Classical_Texts" title="Oxford Classical Texts">Oxford Classical Texts</a> offers the current standard complete Greek text of Plato's complete works. In five volumes edited by <a href="/wiki/John_Burnet_(classicist)" title="John Burnet (classicist)">John Burnet</a>, its first edition was published 1900–1907, and it is still available from the publisher, having last been printed in 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECooper1997xii_&amp;_xxvii_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECooper1997xii_&amp;_xxvii-196">[183]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[184]</a></sup> The second edition is still in progress with only the first volume, printed in 1995, and the <i>Republic</i>, printed in 2003, available. The <i>Cambridge Greek and Latin Texts</i> and <i>Cambridge Classical Texts and Commentaries</i> series includes Greek editions of the <i>Protagoras</i>, <i>Symposium</i>, <i>Phaedrus</i>, <i>Alcibiades</i>, and <i>Clitophon</i>, with English philological, literary, and, to an extent, philosophical commentary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[185]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-199"><a href="#cite_note-199">[186]</a></sup> One distinguished edition of the Greek text is <a href="/wiki/E._R._Dodds" title="E. R. Dodds">E. R. Dodds</a>' of the <i>Gorgias</i>, which includes extensive English commentary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEIrwin1979vi_&amp;_11_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEIrwin1979vi_&amp;_11-200">[187]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDodds1959_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDodds1959-201">[188]</a></sup>
</p><p>The modern standard complete English edition is the 1997 <a href="/wiki/Hackett_Publishing_Company" title="Hackett Publishing Company">Hackett</a> <i>Plato, Complete Works</i>, edited by John M. Cooper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEFine1999a482_202-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFine1999a482-202">[189]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[190]</a></sup> For many of these translations Hackett offers separate volumes which include more by way of commentary, notes, and introductory material. There is also the <i>Clarendon Plato Series</i> by Oxford University Press which offers English translations and thorough philosophical commentary by leading scholars on a few of Plato's works, including <a href="/wiki/John_McDowell" title="John McDowell">John McDowell</a>'s version of the <i>Theaetetus</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[191]</a></sup> Cornell University Press has also begun the <i>Agora</i> series of English translations of classical and medieval philosophical texts, including a few of Plato's.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-205"><a href="#cite_note-205">[192]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Criticism">Criticism</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+link+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+style+.ambox,.mw-parser-output .ambox+.mw-empty-elt+link+link+.ambox{margin-top:-1px}html body.mediawiki .mw-parser-output .ambox.mbox-small-left{margin:4px 1em 4px 0;overflow:hidden;width:238px;border-collapse:collapse;font-size:88%;line-height:1.25em}.mw-parser-output .ambox-speedy{border-left:10px solid #b32424;background-color:#fee7e6}.mw-parser-output .ambox-delete{border-left:10px solid #b32424}.mw-parser-output .ambox-content{border-left:10px solid #f28500}.mw-parser-output .ambox-style{border-left:10px solid #fc3}.mw-parser-output .ambox-move{border-left:10px solid #9932cc}.mw-parser-output .ambox-protection{border-left:10px solid #a2a9b1}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-text{border:none;padding:0.25em 0.5em;width:100%}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image{border:none;padding:2px 0 2px 0.5em;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-imageright{border:none;padding:2px 0.5em 2px 0;text-align:center}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-empty-cell{border:none;padding:0;width:1px}.mw-parser-output .ambox .mbox-image-div{width:52px}html.client-js body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Plato&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">October 2021</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The most famous criticism of the Theory of Forms is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Third_Man_Argument" title="Third Man Argument">Third Man Argument</a> by Aristotle in the <i>Metaphysics</i>. Plato had actually already considered this objection with the idea of "large" rather than "man," in the dialogue <i><a href="/wiki/Parmenides_(dialogue)" title="Parmenides (dialogue)">Parmenides</a></i>, using the elderly Elean philosophers <a href="/wiki/Parmenides" title="Parmenides">Parmenides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeno_of_Elea" title="Zeno of Elea">Zeno</a> characters anachronistically to criticize the character of the younger Socrates who proposed it. The dialogue ends in <i>aporia</i>.
</p><p>Many recent philosophers have diverged from what some would describe as the <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">ontological</a> models and <a href="/wiki/Moral" title="Moral">moral</a> ideals characteristic of traditional Platonism. A number of these postmodern philosophers have thus appeared to disparage Platonism from more or less informed perspectives. <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Nietzsche" title="Friedrich Nietzsche">Friedrich Nietzsche</a> notoriously attacked Plato's "idea of the good itself" along with many fundamentals of Christian morality, which he interpreted as "Platonism for the masses" in one of his most important works, <i><a href="/wiki/Beyond_Good_and_Evil" title="Beyond Good and Evil">Beyond Good and Evil</a></i> (1886). <a href="/wiki/Martin_Heidegger" title="Martin Heidegger">Martin Heidegger</a> argued against Plato's alleged obfuscation of <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Being" title="Being">Being</a></i> in his incomplete tome, <i><a href="/wiki/Being_and_Time" title="Being and Time">Being and Time</a></i> (1927), and the philosopher of science <a href="/wiki/Karl_Popper" title="Karl Popper">Karl Popper</a> argued in the first volume of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Open_Society_and_Its_Enemies" title="The Open Society and Its Enemies">The Open Society and Its Enemies</a></i> (1945) that Plato's alleged proposal for a <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopian</a> political regime in the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Republic_(Plato)" title="The Republic (Plato)">Republic</a></i> was prototypically <a href="/wiki/Totalitarianism" title="Totalitarianism">totalitarian</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_arts">In the arts</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1052" data-file-width="804" decoding="async" height="262" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/200px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/300px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg/400px-Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sanzio_01_Plato_Aristotle.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Plato (left) and Aristotle (right) a detail of <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i>, a fresco by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a>. Aristotle gestures to the earth while holding a copy of his <i><a href="/wiki/Nicomachean_Ethics" title="Nicomachean Ethics">Nicomachean Ethics</a></i> in his hand. Plato holds his <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i> and gestures to the heavens.</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_Academy_mosaic" title="Plato's Academy mosaic">Plato's Academy mosaic</a> was created in the villa of T. Siminius Stephanus in <a href="/wiki/Pompeii" title="Pompeii">Pompeii</a>, around 100 BC to 100 CE. <i><a href="/wiki/The_School_of_Athens" title="The School of Athens">The School of Athens</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Fresco" title="Fresco">fresco</a> by <a href="/wiki/Raphael" title="Raphael">Raphael</a> features Plato also as a central figure. The <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_Chronicle" title="Nuremberg Chronicle">Nuremberg Chronicle</a> depicts Plato and others as anachronistic schoolmen.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_philosophy">In philosophy</span></h3>
<p>Plato's thought is often compared with that of his most famous student, <a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a>, whose reputation during the Western <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> so completely eclipsed that of Plato that the <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholastic</a> philosophers referred to Aristotle as "the Philosopher". However, the study of Plato continued in the <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islamic_Golden_Age" title="Islamic Golden Age">the Caliphates during the Islamic Golden Age</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Golden_age_of_Jewish_culture_in_Spain" title="Golden age of Jewish culture in Spain">Spain during Golden age of Jewish culture</a>.
