</p><p>The only Platonic work known to western scholarship was <i><a href="/wiki/Timaeus_(dialogue)" title="Timaeus (dialogue)">Timaeus</a></i>, until translations were made after the fall of <a href="/wiki/Constantinople" title="Constantinople">Constantinople</a>, which occurred during 1453.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-206"><a href="#cite_note-206">[193]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/George_Gemistos_Plethon" title="George Gemistos Plethon">George Gemistos Plethon</a> brought Plato's original writings from Constantinople in the century of its fall. It is believed that Plethon passed a copy of the <i>Dialogues</i> to <a href="/wiki/Cosimo_de%27_Medici" title="Cosimo de' Medici">Cosimo de' Medici</a> when in 1438 the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Council_of_Ferrara" title="Council of Ferrara">Council of Ferrara</a>, called to unify the Greek and Latin Churches, was adjourned to Florence, where Plethon then lectured on the relation and differences of Plato and Aristotle, and fired Cosimo with his enthusiasm;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELackner200121_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELackner200121-207">[194]</a></sup> Cosimo would supply <a href="/wiki/Marsilio_Ficino" title="Marsilio Ficino">Marsilio Ficino</a> with Plato's text for translation to Latin. During the early Islamic era, <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persian</a>, Arab, and Jewish scholars translated much of Plato into Arabic and wrote <a href="/wiki/Close_reading" title="Close reading">commentaries</a> and interpretations on Plato's, Aristotle's and other Platonist philosophers' works (see <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Avicenna" title="Avicenna">Avicenna</a>, <a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a>). Plato is also referenced by Jewish philosopher and Talmudic scholar <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a> in his <i><a href="/wiki/The_Guide_for_the_Perplexed" title="The Guide for the Perplexed">The Guide for the Perplexed</a></i>. Many of these commentaries on Plato were translated from Arabic into Latin and as such influenced Medieval scholastic philosophers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-208"><a href="#cite_note-208">[195]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a>, with the general resurgence of interest in classical civilization, knowledge of Plato's philosophy would become widespread again in the West. Many of the greatest early modern scientists and artists who broke with <a href="/wiki/Scholasticism" title="Scholasticism">Scholasticism</a> and fostered the flowering of the Renaissance, with the support of the Plato-inspired <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_de%27_Medici" title="Lorenzo de' Medici">Lorenzo</a> (grandson of Cosimo), saw Plato's philosophy as the basis for progress in the arts and sciences. More problematic was Plato's belief in metempsychosis as well as his ethical views (on <a href="/wiki/Polyamory" title="Polyamory">polyamory</a> and <a href="/wiki/Euthanasia" title="Euthanasia">euthanasia</a> in particular), which did not match those of Christianity. It was Plethon's student <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Basilios_Bessarion" title="Basilios Bessarion">Bessarion</a> who reconciled Plato with Christian theology, arguing that Plato's views were only ideals, unattainable due to the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_man" title="Fall of man">fall of man</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-209"><a href="#cite_note-209">[196]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Platonists" title="Cambridge Platonists">Cambridge Platonists</a> were an influential group active in the 17th century. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Carrigan_2012_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-Carrigan_2012-210">[197]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the 19th century, Plato's reputation was restored, and at least on par with Aristotle's. Notable Western philosophers have continued to draw upon Plato's work since that time. Plato's influence has been especially strong in mathematics and the sciences. Plato's resurgence further inspired some of the greatest advances in logic since Aristotle, primarily through <a href="/wiki/Gottlob_Frege" title="Gottlob Frege">Gottlob Frege</a> and his followers <a href="/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del" title="Kurt Gödel">Kurt Gödel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alonzo_Church" title="Alonzo Church">Alonzo Church</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alfred_Tarski" title="Alfred Tarski">Alfred Tarski</a>. <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">Albert Einstein</a> suggested that the scientist who takes philosophy seriously would have to avoid systematization and take on many different roles, and possibly appear as a Platonist or Pythagorean, in that such a one would have "the viewpoint of logical simplicity as an indispensable and effective tool of his research."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinstein1949683–684_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinstein1949683–684-211">[198]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Werner_Heisenberg" title="Werner Heisenberg">Werner Heisenberg</a> stated that “My mind was formed by studying philosophy, Plato and that sort of thing".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[199]</a></sup> and that "Modern physics has definitely decided in favor of Plato. In fact the smallest units of matter are not physical objects in the ordinary sense; they are forms, ideas which can be expressed unambiguously only in mathematical language"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-213"><a href="#cite_note-213">[200]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Taylor_Coleridge" title="Samuel Taylor Coleridge">Samuel Taylor Coleridge</a> said: Everybody is born either a Platonist or an Aristotelian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-214"><a href="#cite_note-214">[201]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="501" decoding="async" height="351" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg/220px-Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg/330px-Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3b/Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg/440px-Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Herma_of_Plato_-_0042MC.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"The safest general characterization of the European philosophical tradition is that it consists of a series of footnotes to Plato." (Alfred North Whitehead, <i><a href="/wiki/Process_and_Reality" title="Process and Reality">Process and Reality</a></i>, 1929).</div></div></div>
<p>The political philosopher and professor <a href="/wiki/Leo_Strauss" title="Leo Strauss">Leo Strauss</a> is considered by some as the prime thinker involved in the recovery of Platonic thought in its more political, and less metaphysical, form. Strauss' political approach was in part inspired by the appropriation of Plato and Aristotle by medieval <a href="/wiki/Jewish_philosophy" title="Jewish philosophy">Jewish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Islamic_philosophy" title="Islamic philosophy">Islamic</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_philosophy" title="Political philosophy">political philosophers</a>, especially <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">Maimonides</a> and <a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>, as opposed to the Christian metaphysical tradition that developed from <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>. Deeply influenced by Nietzsche and Heidegger, Strauss nonetheless rejects their condemnation of Plato and looks to the dialogues for a solution to what all three latter-day thinkers acknowledge as 'the crisis of the West.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/W._V._O._Quine" title="W. V. O. Quine">W. V. O. Quine</a> dubbed the problem of negative existentials "<a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_beard" title="Plato's beard">Plato's beard</a>". <a href="/wiki/Noam_Chomsky" title="Noam Chomsky">Noam Chomsky</a> dubbed the problem of knowledge <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plato%27s_problem" title="Plato's problem">Plato's problem</a>. One author calls the <a href="/wiki/Definist_fallacy" title="Definist fallacy">definist fallacy</a> the Socratic fallacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-215"><a href="#cite_note-215">[202]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Writing_better_articles#Stay_on_topic" title="Wikipedia:Writing better articles"><span title="The material near this tag may contain information that is not relevant to the article's main topic. (April 2020)">relevant?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>More broadly, platonism (sometimes distinguished from Plato's particular view by the lowercase) refers to the view that there are many abstract objects. Still to this day, platonists take number and the truths of mathematics as the best support in favour of this view. Most mathematicians think, like platonists, that numbers and the truths of mathematics are perceived by reason rather than the senses yet exist independently of minds and people, that is to say, they are discovered rather than invented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-216"><a href="#cite_note-216">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>Contemporary platonism is also more open to the idea of there being infinitely many abstract objects, as numbers or propositions might qualify as abstract objects, while ancient Platonism seemed to resist this view, possibly because of the need to overcome the problem of "the One and the Many". Thus e. g. in the Parmenides dialogue, Plato denies there are Forms for more mundane things like hair and mud. However, he repeatedly does support the idea that there are Forms of artifacts, e. g. the Form of Bed. Contemporary platonism also tends to view abstract objects as unable to cause anything, but it is unclear whether the ancient Platonists felt this way.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1045330069" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="sidebar nomobile nowraplinks" style="width:auto;text-align:left;"><tbody><tr><td class="sidebar-pretitle"><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:The_Wikipedia_Library" title="Wikipedia:The Wikipedia Library">Library resources</a> about <br/> <b>Plato</b> <hr/></td></tr><tr><td class="sidebar-content plainlist">

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ancient_scholarship">Ancient scholarship</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Opus" title="Philip of Opus">Philip of Opus</a>, Plato's <a href="/wiki/Amanuensis" title="Amanuensis">amanuensis</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aristotle" title="Aristotle">Aristotle</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Aristonymus" title="Aristonymus">Aristonymus</a>, Plato's friend and student whom he sent in his stead as lawgiver of <a href="/wiki/Megalopolis,_Greece" title="Megalopolis, Greece">Megalopolis</a> in Arcadia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Python_of_Aenus" title="Python of Aenus">Python</a> and <a href="/wiki/Heraclides_of_Aenus" title="Heraclides of Aenus">Heraclides of Aenus</a>, students of Plato who assassinated the tyrannical ruler of <a href="/wiki/Thrace" title="Thrace">Thrace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cotys_I_(Odrysian)" title="Cotys I (Odrysian)">Cotys I</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Speusippus" title="Speusippus">Speusippus</a>, Plato's nephew and the second scholarch of the Academy</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Menedemus_of_Pyrrha" title="Menedemus of Pyrrha">Menedemus of Pyrrha</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Xenocrates" title="Xenocrates">Xenocrates</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crantor" title="Crantor">Crantor</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Polemon_(scholarch)" title="Polemon (scholarch)">Polemon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Crates_of_Athens" title="Crates of Athens">Crates of Athens</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Arcesilaus" title="Arcesilaus">Arcesilaus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carneades" title="Carneades">Carneades</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a>, founder of Neoplatonism, although he had no connection to the previous Academy of Plato</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ammonius_Saccas" title="Ammonius Saccas">Ammonius Saccas</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thrasyllus_of_Mendes" title="Thrasyllus of Mendes">Thrasyllus of Mendes</a>, editor of Plato's works</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_scholarship">Medieval scholarship</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Yahya_Ibn_al-Batriq" title="Yahya Ibn al-Batriq">Yahya Ibn al-Batriq</a>, Syrian scholar and associate of <a href="/wiki/Al-Kindi" title="Al-Kindi">Al-Kindi</a> who translated <i>Timaeus</i> into Arabic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hunayn_ibn_Ishaq" title="Hunayn ibn Ishaq">Hunayn ibn Ishaq</a>, Arab scholar who either amended or surpassed the <i>Timaeus</i> of al-Batriq and translated Plato's <i>Republic</i> and <i>Laws</i> into Arabic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ishaq_ibn_Hunayn" title="Ishaq ibn Hunayn">Ishaq ibn Hunayn</a>, translated Plato's <i>Sophist</i> with the commentary of <a href="/wiki/Olympiodorus_the_Younger" title="Olympiodorus the Younger">Olympiodorus the Younger</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yahya_ibn_Adi" title="Yahya ibn Adi">Yahya ibn Adi</a>, translated <i>Laws</i> into Arabic</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Al-Farabi" title="Al-Farabi">Al-Farabi</a>, author of a commentary on Plato's political philosophy</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Averroes" title="Averroes">Averroes</a>, author of a commentary on the <i>Republic</i></li></ul>

<li><a href="/wiki/Johann_Gottfried_Stallbaum" title="Johann Gottfried Stallbaum">Johann Gottfried Stallbaum</a>, major Plato scholar and commentator in <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a></li>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span></h3>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Oxyrhynchus_Papyri" title="Oxyrhynchus Papyri">Oxyrhynchus Papyri</a>, including the <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Oxyrhynchus_228" title="Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 228">Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 228</a>, containing the oldest fragment of the <a href="/wiki/Laches_(dialogue)" title="Laches (dialogue)"><i>Laches</i></a>, and <a href="/wiki/Papyrus_Oxyrhynchus_24" title="Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 24">Papyrus Oxyrhynchus 24</a>, that of the Book X of the <a href="/wiki/Republic_(Plato)" title="Republic (Plato)"><i>Republic</i></a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plato%27s_Dream" title="Plato's Dream">Plato's Dream</a>, a story written in the 18th century by the French philosopher and satirist <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Plato_(crater)" title="Plato (crater)">Plato</a>, a lunar impact crater on the <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">Moon</a> aged 3.8 billion years, named after the Greek philosopher</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/PLATO_(spacecraft)" title="PLATO (spacecraft)">PLATO (spacecraft)</a>, a proposed <a href="/wiki/Space_telescope" title="Space telescope">space telescope</a> under development by the <a href="/wiki/European_Space_Agency" title="European Space Agency">European Space Agency</a> for launch in 2026, named after the Greek philosopher</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap mw-references-columns"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"...the subject of philosophy, as it is often conceived — a rigorous and systematic examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethical" title="Ethical">ethical</a>, political, <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">metaphysical</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">epistemological</a> issues, armed with a distinctive method — can be called his invention."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SEP_3-0"><a href="#cite_note-SEP-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Though influenced primarily by Socrates, to the extent that Socrates is usually the main character in many of Plato's writings, he was also influenced by Heraclitus, Parmenides, and the Pythagoreans"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto-8">[7]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-21"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-21">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Diogenes Laërtius mentions that Plato "was born, according to some writers, in Aegina in the house of Phidiades, the son of Thales." Diogenes mentions as one of his sources the <i>Universal History</i> of <a href="/wiki/Favorinus" title="Favorinus">Favorinus</a>. According to Favorinus, Ariston, Plato's family, and his family were sent by Athens to settle as <a href="/wiki/Cleruchy" title="Cleruchy">cleruchs</a> (colonists retaining their Athenian citizenship), on the island of Aegina, from which the Spartans expelled them after Plato's birth there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaIII_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaIII-17">[15]</a></sup> Nails points out, however, that there is no record of any Spartan expulsion of Athenians from Aegina between 431–411 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails200254_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails200254-18">[16]</a></sup> On the other hand, at the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Nicias" title="Peace of Nicias">Peace of Nicias</a>, Aegina was silently left under Athens' control, and it was not until the summer of 411 that the Spartans overran the island.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Th5.18.8.92_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Th5.18.8.92-19">[17]</a></sup> Therefore, Nails concludes that "perhaps Ariston was a cleruch, perhaps he went to Aegina in 431, and perhaps Plato was born on Aegina, but none of this enables a precise dating of Ariston's death (or Plato's birth).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails200254_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails200254-18">[16]</a></sup> Aegina is regarded as Plato's place of birth by the Suda as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Suda_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-Suda-20">[18]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-27"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-27">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Apollodorus of Athens said Plato was born on the seventh day of the month <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Thargelion&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Thargelion (page does not exist)">Thargelion</a>; according to this tradition the god <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> was born this day.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaII_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaII-22">[19]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Platonist" title="Platonist">Platonists</a> celebrated Plato's birth on November 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTENails20061_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTENails20061-23">[20]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Ulrich_von_Wilamowitz-Moellendorff" title="Ulrich von Wilamowitz-Moellendorff">Ulrich von Wilamowitz-Moellendorff</a> estimates that Plato was born when Diotimos was <a href="/wiki/Archon#Ancient_Greece" title="Archon">archon eponymous</a>, namely between July 29, 428 BC and July 24, 427 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilamowitz-Moellendorff200546_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilamowitz-Moellendorff200546-24">[21]</a></sup> Greek philologist Ioannis Kalitsounakis believes that he was born on May 26 or 27, 427 BC.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Britannica_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Britannica-25">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-26">[23]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-35"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-35">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">According to <a href="/wiki/James_Adam_(classicist)" title="James Adam (classicist)">James Adam</a>, some have held that "Glaucon and Adeimantus were uncles of Plato, but <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Zeller" title="Eduard Zeller">Zeller</a> decides for the usual view that they were brothers."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[30]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-49"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-49">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">From <i><a href="/wiki/Arete" title="Arete">aristos</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kleos" title="Kleos">kleos</a></i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-66"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-66">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">A scroll by <a href="/wiki/Philodemus" title="Philodemus">Philodemus</a> analysed in 2019 may suggest that Plato was enslaved earlier than was previously believed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[59]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">He regarded "logistic" as appropriate for business men and men of war who "must learn the art of numbers or he will not know how to array his troops," while "arithmetic" was appropriate for philosophers "because he has to arise out of the sea of change and lay hold of true being."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[71]</a></sup></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-131"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-131">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a> describes this in the last part of his final <i><a href="/wiki/Enneads" title="Enneads">Ennead</a></i> (VI, 9) entitled <i>On the Good, or the One</i> (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">Περὶ τἀγαθοῦ ἢ τοῦ ἑνός</span></span>). Jens Halfwassen states in <a class="external text" href="http://ccat.sas.upenn.edu/bmcr/2006/2006-08-16.html" rel="nofollow"><i>Der Aufstieg zum Einen'</i></a><i> (2006) that "Plotinus' ontology—which should be called Plotinus' <a href="/wiki/Henology" title="Henology">henology</a>—is a rather accurate philosophical renewal and continuation of Plato's unwritten doctrine, i.e. the doctrine rediscovered by Krämer and Gaiser."</i></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In one of his letters (Epistolae 1612) <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ficino" title="Ficino">Ficino</a> writes: "The main goal of the divine Plato ... is to show one principle of things, which he called the One (<span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">τὸ ἕν</span></span>)", cf. <a href="#CITEREFMontoriola1926">Montoriola 1926</a>, p. 147.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-135"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-135">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For a brief description of the problem see for example <a href="#CITEREFGaiser1980">Gaiser 1980</a>. A more detailed analysis is given by <a href="#CITEREFKrämer1990">Krämer 1990</a>. Another description is by <a href="#CITEREFReale1997">Reale 1997</a> and <a href="#CITEREFReale1990">Reale 1990</a>. A thorough analysis of the consequences of such an approach is given by <a href="#CITEREFSzlezak1999">Szlezak 1999</a>. Another supporter of this interpretation is the German philosopher <a href="/wiki/Karl_Albert" title="Karl Albert">Karl Albert</a>, cf. <a href="#CITEREFAlbert1980">Albert 1980</a> or <a href="#CITEREFAlbert1996">Albert 1996</a>. <a href="/wiki/Hans-Georg_Gadamer" title="Hans-Georg Gadamer">Hans-Georg Gadamer</a> is also sympathetic towards it, cf. <a href="#CITEREFGrondin2010">Grondin 2010</a> and <a href="#CITEREFGadamer1980">Gadamer 1980</a>. Gadamer's final position on the subject is stated in <a href="#CITEREFGadamer1997">Gadamer 1997</a>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-147"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-147">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Some use the term <i>allegory</i> instead of myth. This is in accordance with the practice in the specialized literature, in which it is common to find that the terms allegory and myth are used as synonyms. Nevertheless, there is a trend among modern scholars to use the term <i>myth</i> and avoid the term allegory, as it is considered more appropriate to the modern interpretation of Plato's writings. One of the first to initiate this trend was the Oxford University professor <a href="/wiki/John_Alexander_Stewart_(philosopher)" title="John Alexander Stewart (philosopher)">John Alexander Stewart</a>, in his work <i>The Myths of Plato</i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-158"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-158">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The time is not long after the death of Socrates; for the Pythagoreans [Echecrates &amp; co.] have not heard any details yet".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-157"><a href="#cite_note-157">[145]</a></sup></span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<li id="cite_note-145"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-145">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFChappel">Chappel, Timothy. <a class="external text" href="https://www.academia.edu/10450689" rel="nofollow">"Mythos and Logos in Plato"</a>. Open University<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">20 August</span> 2017</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.atitle=Mythos+and+Logos+in+Plato&amp;rft.aulast=Chappel&amp;rft.aufirst=Timothy&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fwww.academia.edu%2F10450689&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APlato"></span> <span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment"><code class="cs1-code">{{<a href="/wiki/Template:Cite_journal" title="Template:Cite journal">cite journal</a>}}</code>: </span><span class="cs1-hidden-error citation-comment">Cite journal requires <code class="cs1-code">|journal=</code> (<a href="/wiki/Help:CS1_errors#missing_periodical" title="Help:CS1 errors">help</a>)</span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For instance, Timaeus' speech in the <i>Timaeus</i> is three times called a <i>eikōs muthos</i> (“a plausible story”) (29d2, 59c6, 68d2); it is seven times called an <i>eikōs logos</i> (“a plausible account”) (30b7, 48d2, 53d5–6, 55d5, 56a1, 57d6, 90e8). For discussions of the importance of this, see Campbell 2022 and Brisson 2012. Brisson, Luc “Why is the <i>Timaeus</i> Called an <i>Eikōs Muthos</i> and an <i>Eikōs Logos</i>?” in C. Collobert, P.     Destrée, and F. J. Gonzalez (eds.), <i>Plato and Myth: Studies on the Use and Status of Platonic Myths.</i> Leiden: Brill, 2012, 369-391. Campbell, Douglas R. "Plato's Theory of Reincarnation: Eschatology and Natural Philosophy," <i>Review of Metaphysics</i> 75 (4): 643-665. 2022. </span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-148"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-148">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFEdelstein1949">Edelstein, Ludwig (October 1949). "The Function of the Myth in Plato's Philosophy". <i>Journal of the History of Ideas</i>. <b>X</b> (4): 463–481. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.2307%2F2707185" rel="nofollow">10.2307/2707185</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/JSTOR_(identifier)" title="JSTOR (identifier)">JSTOR</a> <a class="external text" href="//www.jstor.org/stable/2707185" rel="nofollow">2707185</a>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Journal+of+the+History+of+Ideas&amp;rft.atitle=The+Function+of+the+Myth+in+Plato%27s+Philosophy&amp;rft.volume=X&amp;rft.issue=4&amp;rft.pages=463-481&amp;rft.date=1949-10&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.2307%2F2707185&amp;rft_id=%2F%2Fwww.jstor.org%2Fstable%2F2707185%23id-name%3DJSTOR&amp;rft.aulast=Edelstein&amp;rft.aufirst=Ludwig&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APlato"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-149"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-149">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For instance, Chad Jorgensen argues that Plato's eschatological accounts are "much better suited to a creative discourse aimed at capturing the imagination of a particular audience than to an attempt to describe an independently existing reality." Jorgensen, Chad. <i>The Embodied Soul in Plato’s Later Thought</i>. Cambridge: Cambridge   University Press, 2018. The quotation is from page 199.</span>

<li id="cite_note-209"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-209">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation web cs1" id="CITEREFHarris2002">Harris, Jonathan (2002). <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20030930225420/http://www.the-orb.net/encyclop/late/laterbyz/harris-ren.html" rel="nofollow">"Byzantines in Renaissance Italy"</a>. <i>ORB: The Online Reference Book for Medieval Studies</i>. College of Staten Island, City University of New York. Archived from <a class="external text" href="http://the-orb.net/encyclop/late/laterbyz/harris-ren.html" rel="nofollow">the original</a> on 30 September 2003<span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">9 February</span> 2015</span>.</cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=ORB%3A+The+Online+Reference+Book+for+Medieval+Studies&amp;rft.atitle=Byzantines+in+Renaissance+Italy&amp;rft.date=2002&amp;rft.aulast=Harris&amp;rft.aufirst=Jonathan&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fthe-orb.net%2Fencyclop%2Flate%2Flaterbyz%2Fharris-ren.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APlato"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-Carrigan_2012-210"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Carrigan_2012_210-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation encyclopaedia cs1" id="CITEREFCarrigan2012">Carrigan, Henry L. Jr. (2012) [2011]. "Cambridge Platonists". <i>The Encyclopedia of Christian Civilization</i>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chichester,_West_Sussex" title="Chichester, West Sussex">Chichester, West Sussex</a>: <a href="/wiki/Wiley-Blackwell" title="Wiley-Blackwell">Wiley-Blackwell</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1002%2F9780470670606.wbecc0219" rel="nofollow">10.1002/9780470670606.wbecc0219</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a> <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/9781405157629" title="Special:BookSources/9781405157629"><bdi>9781405157629</bdi></a>. <q>The Cambridge Platonists were an influential group of 17th century philosophers and clergy whose teachings on philosophy and theology later influenced Leibniz, Locke, the Scottish common sense philosopher Thomas Reid, and Kant. They thrived at Cambridge largely between 1633 and 1678, though their younger disciples carried their thinking into the early 18th century. They identified themselves neither with the Puritans nor the High Anglicans, and they encouraged toleration within the church. The Cambridge Platonists drew on the dualistic philosophies of mind and matter of Plato (427–347 BCE) and <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a> (205–270 CE), contending that mind is prior to matter and that reason is superior to knowledge derived from the senses. They developed the ideas that reason and morality reside in the human mind and that reason is indeed the indwelling of God in the mind that allows individuals to judge both natural and divine revelation. This group of thinkers, who were among the first to write philosophy primarily in the English language (rather than in Latin or French), believed that the rational and the spiritual could go hand-in-hand. These philosophers taught that human nature possessed an innate tendency toward goodness and that human nature was perfectible. Humans possess free will, and they learn rationally the wisdom of practicing Christ's universal love. Thus, while the Cambridge Platonists devoted their energies to metaphysical questions regarding the immortality of the soul and the existence of God, they also attended closely to morality and the principles of daily living.</q></cite><span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Cambridge+Platonists&amp;rft.btitle=The+Encyclopedia+of+Christian+Civilization&amp;rft.place=Chichester%2C+West+Sussex&amp;rft.pub=Wiley-Blackwell&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1002%2F9780470670606.wbecc0219&amp;rft.isbn=9781405157629&amp;rft.aulast=Carrigan&amp;rft.aufirst=Henry+L.+Jr.&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3APlato"></span></span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-FOOTNOTEEinstein1949683–684-211"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-FOOTNOTEEinstein1949683–684_211-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><a href="#CITEREFEinstein1949">Einstein 1949</a>, pp. 683–684.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-212"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-212">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1067248974" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><cite class="citation journal cs1" id="CITEREFDe_Haro2020">De Haro, Sebastian (2020).
